Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Principle: Ikhtilaf of Scholars Brings Ease
Stating the principle and its application to purificatory rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that scholarly difference brings ease and apply it to the rulings on used water and animal dung.

Core Idea
Scholarly difference is not confusion but a source of ease, leading to lighter rulings in cases of necessity.

This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: 'Ikhtilaf al-ulama yurithu takhfifan' — the difference of scholars brings ease. You will see how this principle is used to adopt the lighter ruling on used water and animal dung, despite internal and inter-madhhab disagreements. Understanding this principle will help you navigate ikhtilaf within and between schools, and appreciate its practical role in fatwa-giving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the key maxim in Arabic and English: 'Inna ikhtilāf al-ʿulamāʾ yūrithu takhfīfan'. Understand that ikhtilaf is not a problem but a mercy that permits leniency.
2
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Used Water
Recognize that Imam Abu Hanifa considered used water heavily impure (najīs ghalīẓ) due to a hadith, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad considered it lightly impure (najīs khafīf).
The hadith from Ibn Masʿūd cited by Abu Hanifa did not convince the Ṣāḥibayn because it lacked strength to overrule the principle of ease.
3
Apply the Principle to Used Water
See that the author of al-Hidayah uses the maxim to prefer the lighter view: because scholars differ, ease is produced, so used water is merely lightly impure.
This is a classic example of takhfīf due to ikhtilāf.
4
Extend to Animal Dung
Notice the same logic applies to animal dung (arwāth): even though Abu Hanifa considered it heavy due to a text, the Ṣāḥibayn rule it light because of ikhtilaf and common affliction (balwā).
Necessity (ḍarūrah) also plays a role; roads are filled with dung, making it hard to avoid.
5
Conclude the Muftā Bihī
Understand that the fatwa (operative ruling) today follows the lighter view for both used water and dung, based on the strength of the maxim and the need for ease.
This is a ruling from the late authorities (fatawā mutākhkhirīn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ جلد اول
(٢٠) إِنَّ اخْتِلَافَ الْعُلَمَاءِ يُوْرِثُ تَخْفِيفًا.
ترجمہ: علماء کا اختلاف تخفیف پیدا کرتا ہے۔
تشریح: امام ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک ماء مستعمل نجس تو ہے لیکن نجاست خفیفہ کے حکم میں ہے نہ کہ نجاست غلیظہ کے حکم میں ، امام حسن بن زیاد رحمہ اللہ نے امام ابوحنیفہ رحمہ اللہ سے نجاست غلیظہ کا قول نقل کیا ہے، جب کہ امام مالک رحمہ اللہ کے ہاں یہ طاہر اور مطہر ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل کے پیش کیا ہے کہ ماء مستعمل کی طہارت اور نجاست میں علماء کا اختلاف ہے اور اختلاف علماء کی وجہ سے تخفیف پیدا ہو جاتی ہے، لہذا ماء مستعمل نجاست خفیفہ کے حکم میں ہو گا نہ کہ نجاست غلیظہ کے ۔
(۲۱) إِنَّ الشَّيْءَ إِذَا ثَبَتَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ يَتَقَدَّرُ بِقَدْرِهَا .
الهداية كتاب الطهارات، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۸، ط: رحمانیه لمكان الاختلاف) أى لأجل اختلاف العلماء في الماء المستعمل، فإن عند مالک طاهر وطهور كما ذكرنا ، واختلاف العلماء يورث الإخفاف.
البناية: كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۴۰۱، ط: دار الكتب العلمية وثمرة الاختلاف تظهر فى الأرواث: عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله نجاستها غليظة؛ لأنه ورد فيه النص، وهو حديث ابن مسعود رضى الله عنه على ما روينا، ولم يعارض هذا الحديث نص آخر فيتغلظ وعندهما نجاستها خفيفة لاختلاف العلماء فيه، ولمكان البلوى، فإن الطرق مملوءة فيها، وقد يحتاج الإنسان إلى سوق الدواب فيمشى عليها، فيصيبه الروث على وجه لا يمكنه الدفع عن نفسه وللضرورة أثر فى إسقاط النجاسة كما في سؤر الهرة، فلأن يكون لها أثر فى التخفيف أولى وقد ذكرنا رواية المعلى عن محمد رحمه الله أنه قال: الروث لا يمنع جواز الصلاة وإن كان كثيراً فاحشاً. ونجاسة يول ما يؤكل لحمه على قول من يقول بنـجـاسـتـه خفيفة، حتى لو أصاب الثوب لا يمنع جواز الصلاة ما لم يكن كثيراً فاحشاً، وإذا وقع قطرة في الماء أفسده؛ لأن القليل فى الماء يصير كثيراً، وإنما كانت نجاسته خفيفة إما
لأن بنجاسته اختلاف، فتخفف نجاسته، أو لأن فيه ضرورة وللضرورة أثر في التخفيف. المحيط البرهاني كتاب الطهارة، الفصل السابع، ج ۱ ص ۱۹۴، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
الهداية كتاب الطهارات، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۸، ط: رحمانیه
The difference of scholars brings ease. Translation: The difference of scholars brings ease. Explanation: According to Imam Abu Yusuf, used water is impure but in the category of light impurity, not heavy impurity. Imam Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa the ruling of heavy impurity. Whereas Imam Malik holds it is pure and purifying. The author of al-Hidayah presented this principle as evidence: there is difference among scholars regarding the purity and impurity of used water, and due to difference of scholars, ease is produced, so used water takes the ruling of light impurity, not heavy impurity. (21) When something is established by necessity, it is measured by its extent. Al-Hidayah: Book of Purification, Chapter of Water Permissible for Wudu, vol. 1 p. 38, Rahmaniyyah edition: 'Due to the difference' i.e. because of the difference of scholars concerning used water, for according to Malik it is pure and purifying as we mentioned, and difference of scholars produces lightness. Al-Binayah: Book of Purification, Chapter of Water Permissible for Wudu, vol. 1 p. 401, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah: 'And the fruit of difference appears in the case of dung: according to Abu Hanifa, its impurity is heavy because there is textual evidence (hadith of Ibn Mas'ud) and no contradictory text, so it is heavy. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), its impurity is light due to difference of scholars and due to common affliction, because roads are filled with it, and a person may need to drive animals and walk on it, and dung may get on him in a way he cannot avoid. And necessity has effect in removing impurity, as in the case of the leftover of a cat, so it is more fitting that it has effect in lightening. And we have mentioned the narration of al-Mu'alla from Muhammad that he said: Dung does not prevent the validity of prayer even if it is much and excessive. And the impurity of urine of an animal whose meat is eaten, according to those who say it is light, is such that if it gets on clothing it does not prevent prayer unless it is much and excessive. And if a drop falls into water, it spoils it because a small amount in water becomes much. And its impurity is light either because its impurity is subject to difference, so its impurity is lightened, or because there is necessity and necessity has effect in lightening. Al-Muhit al-Burhani: Book of Purification, Chapter Seven, vol. 1 p. 194, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah. Al-Hidayah: Book of Purification, Chapter of Water Permissible for Wudu, vol. 1 p. 38, Rahmaniyyah edition.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents two Hanafi legal principles and their applications. The first principle (20) is that scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf al-ulama) brings ease (takhfif). The author applies this to used water: because scholars differ on its ruling, the Hanafi school adopts the lighter view that it is only lightly impure (najis khafifah), not heavily impure. The second principle (21) is that when something is established by necessity, it is measured by its extent (tataqaddar bi qadriha). This is illustrated by the example of animal dung (arwath) on roads: due to necessity and common affliction, its impurity is considered light, allowing leniency in prayer. The passage quotes several Hanafi sources including al-Hidayah, al-Binayah, and al-Muhit al-Burhani to support these rulings. The key takeaway is that legal rulings are not absolute but can be lightened based on scholarly difference and necessity.

Key Terms
اختلاف العلماء (ikhtilāf al-'ulamā'): Disagreement among jurists on a ruling
نجاسة خفیفہ (najāsah khafīfah): Light impurity that does not prevent prayer unless excessive

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that used water is only lightly impure (najis khafifah) rather than heavy?
A) Because the Quran explicitly says so.
B) Because of the principle that difference of scholars brings ease.
C) Because all scholars agree on light impurity.
D) Because heavy impurity is not applicable to water.

Key Takeaway
The difference of scholars is not a weakness but a source of ease, allowing the adoption of lighter rulings in cases of necessity.

The Principle of Ikhtilaf Creating Leniency and Its Application to Used Water
Applying ikhtilaf principle to classify used water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) leads to leniency (takhfif) and apply it to determine the degree of impurity of used water.

Core Idea
Scholarly disagreement itself justifies choosing the lighter ruling.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal principle: when scholars differ on a ruling, the existence of ikhtilaf itself calls for leniency (takhfif). You will see this applied to the case of used water (ma' musta'mal), where the Hanafi school prefers the classification of light impurity over severe impurity, even though some narrations from Abu Hanifa and the view of Imam Malik differ. Understanding this principle is crucial for appreciating how the Hanafi school navigates ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Used water (ma' musta'mal). What are the possible rulings? Pure, severe impurity (najasat ghalizah), or light impurity (najasat khafifah).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The principle 'ikhtilaf al-ulama yurithu takhfifan' and the argument of necessity (darurah).
The passage also cites supporting texts from Al-Hidayah, Al-Binayah, and Al-Muhit al-Burhani.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the principle prove leniency? Because ikhtilaf creates doubt or difficulty, so the lighter view is adopted to ease the burden on the community.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Who differs? Imam Malik (pure), a narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad (severe), and Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (light).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is mufta bihi? The light impurity ruling (Abu Yusuf's view) is relied upon because of the principle of takhfif.

Source Text

(٢٠) إِنَّ اخْتِلَافَ الْعُلَمَاءِ يُوْرِثُ تَخْفِيفًا.
ترجمہ: علماء کا اختلاف تخفیف پیدا کرتا ہے۔
تشریح: امام ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک ماء مستعمل نجس تو ہے لیکن نجاست خفیفہ کے حکم میں ہے نہ کہ نجاست غلیظہ کے حکم میں ، امام حسن بن زیاد رحمہ اللہ نے امام ابوحنیفہ رحمہ اللہ سے نجاست غلیظہ کا قول نقل کیا ہے، جب کہ امام مالک رحمہ اللہ کے ہاں یہ طاہر اور مطہر ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل کے پیش کیا ہے کہ ماء مستعمل کی طہارت اور نجاست میں علماء کا اختلاف ہے اور اختلاف علماء کی وجہ سے تخفیف پیدا ہو جاتی ہے، لہذا ماء مستعمل نجاست خفیفہ کے حکم میں ہو گا نہ کہ نجاست غلیظہ کے ۔
الهداية كتاب الطهارات، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۸، ط: رحمانیه لمكان الاختلاف) أى لأجل اختلاف العلماء في الماء المستعمل، فإن عند مالک طاهر وطهور كما ذكرنا ، واختلاف العلماء يورث الإخفاف.
البناية: كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۴۰۱، ط: دار الكتب العلمية وثمرة الاختلاف تظهر فى الأرواث: عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله نجاستها غليظة؛ لأنه ورد فيه النص، وهو حديث ابن مسعود رضى الله عنه على ما روينا، ولم يعارض هذا الحديث نص آخر فيتغلظ وعندهما نجاستها خفيفة لاختلاف العلماء فيه، ولمكان البلوى، فإن الطرق مملوءة فيها، وقد يحتاج الإنسان إلى سوق الدواب فيمشى عليها، فيصيبه الروث على وجه لا يمكنه الدفع عن نفسه وللضرورة أثر فى إسقاط النجاسة كما في سؤر الهرة، فلأن يكون لها أثر فى التخفيف أولى وقد ذكرنا رواية المعلى عن محمد رحمه الله أنه قال: الروث لا يمنع جواز الصلاة وإن كان كثيراً فاحشاً. ونجاسة يول ما يؤكل لحمه على قول من يقول بنـجـاسـتـه خفيفة، حتى لو أصاب الثوب لا يمنع جواز الصلاة ما لم يكن كثيراً فاحشاً، وإذا وقع قطرة في الماء أفسده؛ لأن القليل فى الماء يصير كثيراً، وإنما كانت نجاسته خفيفة إما لأن بنجاسته اختلاف، فتخفف نجاسته، أو لأن فيه ضرورة وللضرورة أثر في التخفيف. المحيط البرهاني كتاب الطهارة، الفصل السابع، ج ۱ ص ۱۹۴، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
الهداية كتاب الطهارات، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۸، ط: رحمانیه
(20) Verily, the difference of scholars produces leniency. Translation: The difference of scholars creates leniency. Explanation: According to Imam Abu Yusuf, used water is impure but in the category of minor impurity, not severe impurity. Imam Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa the statement of severe impurity. While Imam Malik considers it pure and purifying. The author of Al-Hidayah presented this principle as evidence that there is disagreement among scholars regarding the purity and impurity of used water, and due to the difference of scholars, leniency arises. Therefore, used water will be in the category of minor impurity, not severe impurity. [Then Arabic quotes from Al-Hidayah, Al-Binayah, and Al-Muhit al-Burhani elaborating on the application of this principle to dung and water.]
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal principle that differences of opinion among scholars (ikhtilaf) lead to leniency (takhfif) in rulings. It is introduced as a proof to resolve the disagreement regarding used water (ma' musta'mal). The commentary explains that Imam Abu Yusuf considered used water as najis but of light degree (najasat khafifah), while Imam Hasan ibn Ziyad reported from Imam Abu Hanifa that it is severe impurity (najis ghalizah). Imam Malik holds it to be pure and purifying. The author of Al-Hidayah uses this principle to argue that due to the existence of ikhtilaf, the ruling should be lightened, so used water is treated as light impurity. The passage then provides supporting texts from Al-Hidayah, Al-Binayah, and Al-Muhit al-Burhani, which further apply this principle to other matters like animal dung (arwath), where the same leniency is applied due to ikhtilaf and necessity (darurah). This illustrates how the principle is used to prefer the lighter ruling in cases of scholarly disagreement.

Key Terms
ماء مستعمل (ma' musta'mal): Water that has been used for ablution (wudu) or ritual purification, which in the Hanafi school is considered impure (najis) due to contact with the body.
نجاست خفیفہ (najasat khafifah): A category of impurity that is considered light, such that small amounts are excused in prayer (e.g., less than a dirham's area).
نجاست غلیظہ (najasat ghalizah): A severe impurity that requires thorough washing and invalidates prayer if present in any amount.
تخفیف (takhfif): Leniency or lightening of a ruling, especially in matters of impurity due to necessity or difference of opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle 'ikhtilaf al-ulama yurithu takhfifan', which ruling is adopted for used water in the Hanafi school?
A) It is pure and purifying.
B) It is severely impure.
C) It is impure but light impurity.
D) It is pure but not purifying.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of takhfif from ikhtilaf leads to classifying used water as light impurity (najasat khafifah).

Necessity Determines the Extent: When Water Becomes Musta'mal
Using a legal maxim to define the moment water becomes musta'mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal principle of necessity to determine when water used in wudu becomes musta'mal, and distinguish the Hanafi position from other schools.

Core Idea
A ruling granted by necessity ends when the necessity ends; water becomes musta'mal upon separation.

This passage introduces a fundamental legal principle: rulings established due to necessity are limited to the extent of that necessity. The author of Al-Hidayah uses it specifically to determine when water used in wudu becomes musta'mal. Understanding this principle clarifies why water is not considered used while still on the limb but becomes so immediately upon separation—a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the maxim 'al-shay' idha thabata bil-darurati yataqaddaru bi-qadriha'. This means that when a ruling is established due to necessity, it remains only as long as the necessity exists.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) used in Hanafi usul.
2
Apply the Necessity to Wudu
Recognize the necessity in wudu: while water is still on the limb, it is needed for the act of purification; after separation, the need ends.
The necessity is the continuation of the purification act.
3
Derive the Hanafi Ruling
Apply the principle: before separation, the water must not be considered musta'mal (used) because that would hinder the act of wudu. After separation, the necessity ends, so the water takes the ruling of musta'mal.
Separation (infisal) is the key criterion, not collection in a vessel.
4
Confirm the Mufta Bihi Position
The Hanafi school holds that water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separation. The text cites al-Hidayah, al-Binayah, and Tabyeen al-Haqaiq as confirming this view.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Source Text

ترجمہ: جب کوئی شئی ضرورت کی وجہ سے ثابت ہوتو وہ بقدر ضرورت ثابت ہوگی۔
تشریح : اس اصول میں ماء مستعمل کے وقت کا تعین کیا گیا ہے کہ پانی کب مستعمل ہوگا۔ تو صاحب ہدایہ نے اس اصول کو ذکر کر کے ماء مستعمل ہونے کے وقت کے تعین پر بطور دلیل کے پیش کیا ہے کہ احناف کے نزدیک پانی جو نبی بدن سے جدا ہو جائے تو وہ مستعمل ہو جاتا ہے،اس کا کسی برتن یا جگہ میں جمع ہونا مستعمل ہونے کے لئے شرط نہیں ہے ،اس کی دلیل میں اس اصول کو ذکر کیا ہے کہ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ بدن سے جدا ہونے سے پہلے پانی کو استعمال کا حکم ضرورت کی وجہ سے نہیں دیا گیا لیکن جدا ہونے کے بعد کوئی ضرورت نہیں رہتی ، اس لئے پانی بدن سے جدا ہوتے ہی مستعمل ہو جائے گا۔
(لأن سقوط حكم الاستعمال أى سقوط حكم كون الماء مستعملا (قبل الانفصال أى قبل انفصال الماء عن عضو المتوضئ (للضرورة) أى لأجل ضرورة تعذر الاحتراز عنه (ولا ضرورة بعده أى عبد الانفصال وفى "المحيط " أن الماء إنما يأخذ حكم الاستعمال إذا زائل البدن، والاجتماع فى المكان ليس بشرط هذا هو مذهب أصحابنا. البناية: كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذي يجوز به الوضوء، ج ا ص ۴۰۴، ط: دار الكتب العلمية وَالصَّحِيحُ أَنَّهُ كَمَا زَايَلَ الْعُضْوَ يَصِيرُ مُسْتَعْمَلًا، لِأَنَّ سُقُوطَ حُكْمِ الِاسْتِعْمَالِ قَبْلَ الِانْفِصَالِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ وَلَا ضَرُورَةً بَعْدَهُ.
تبيين الحقائق كتاب الطهارة ، الماء المستعمل، ج ا ص ۲۵، ط: المطبعة الكبرى] الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۳۹، ۴۰، ط: رحمانیه
(21) Verily, when a thing is established by necessity, it is measured by its measure. Translation: When something is established due to necessity, it remains only to the extent of the necessity. Explanation: This principle determines the timing of used water. The author of Al-Hidayah cites it to argue that water immediately becomes musta'mal upon separation from the limb; collection in a vessel is not required. The necessity that suspends the ruling of being musta'mal ends after separation. [Then Arabic from al-Binayah, Tabyeen al-Haqaiq, and al-Hidayah confirming this view.]
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the principle that a ruling granted out of necessity is limited to the scope of that necessity. The author of Al-Hidayah uses it to determine when water becomes musta'mal. According to the Hanafi school, water becomes musta'mal immediately upon leaving the limb, even without collecting in a vessel. The necessity is the need to perform wudu; before separation, the water is still part of the ablution and is not considered used. After separation, the necessity ends, so the water takes the ruling of musta'mal. The supporting Arabic texts from al-Binayah, Tabyeen al-Haqaiq, and al-Hidayah confirm that the correct view is that water becomes musta'mal as soon as it separates, and gathering in a place is not a condition.

Key Terms
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity; a situation where avoiding a ruling would cause hardship, allowing leniency.
انفصال (infisal): Separation of water from the limb during wudu; the moment after which water is considered used.
مستعمل (musta'mal): Used water; water that has been in contact with a limb during purification and is no longer suitable for ritual use in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Immediately after it separates from the limb.
C) Only after it has been used for a complete wudu.
D) It never becomes musta'mal.

Key Takeaway
Water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separation from the limb because the necessity that suspended its used status ends at that point.

Beneficial Things Are Not Intrinsically Impure
Rational proof that beneficial items are not intrinsically impure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that beneficial things are not najis al-ayn and apply it to determine the intrinsic purity of animals.

Core Idea
If a thing is lawful to benefit from, it cannot be intrinsically impure.

The Hanafi school uses a rational proof to determine intrinsic purity: anything that can be lawfully benefited from is not intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). This principle is applied to animals like the dog, which is permissible to keep for guarding and hunting. If the dog were intrinsically impure, such benefit would be prohibited; since it is permitted, the dog is pure. By contrast, swine is intrinsically impure because the Quran explicitly declares it rijs (impure).

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Read the Arabic maxim: 'Inna ma yantafi'u bihi fahuwa laysa bi-najis al-ayn.' Understand that it means anything from which lawful benefit is derived is not intrinsically impure.
2
Identify the key example
The text uses the dog to illustrate the principle. The dog is not intrinsically impure because it is permissible to keep it for benefit (guarding, hunting).
The ruling on dog saliva (najis) is separate; here we discuss the substance itself.
3
Analyze the rational proof
If a thing were intrinsically impure, benefiting from it would be prohibited (since the Shari'ah prohibits using impure things without necessity). Since the Shari'ah permits benefiting, the thing cannot be intrinsically impure. This is a qiyas jaliy or istidlal bi al-urf.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli), not scriptural, except for the exception (swine).
4
Contrast with the exception
Swine (khinzir) is explicitly declared rijs (impure) in the Quran (Surah al-An'am 6:145). Therefore, even if one could benefit from it, it remains najis al-ayn because the text overrides the rational principle.
This exception proves the rule: the principle applies where there is no explicit text declaring impurity.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school considers the dog intrinsically impure, arguing from different hadith. The Hanafi school relies on the permissibility of keeping dogs for certain benefits as evidence that the dog itself is not najis al-ayn.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; each school has its proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: جس چیز سے انتفاع حاصل کیا جائے وہ پنجس العین نہیں ہوتی۔
تشریح : اصول کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جن چیزوں سے نفع حاصل کیا جاتا ہے وہ نجس العین نہیں ہوتی ، صاحب ہدایہ نے ایک مثال سے واضح فرمایا ہے کہ جیسے کتا صحیح قول کے مطابق نجس العین نہیں ہے، کیونکہ پہرہ داری اور شکار کرنے کے لئے کتا اپنے پاس رکھنا جائز ہے، پس اگر کتا نجس العین ہوتا تو اس سے نفع لینا شرعاً ممنوع ہوتا، جب اس سے نفع لینا جائز ہے تو وہ نجس العین نہیں ہے، بخلاف خنزیر کے کہ وہ پنجس العین ہے اور اس پر نص ہے (فَإِنَّهُ رِجْسٌ فَإِنَّ “ کی ضمیر کا
(22) Verily, that which is benefited from is not intrinsically impure. Translation: A thing from which benefit is taken is not intrinsically impure. Explanation: The gist of the principle is that things from which benefit is derived are not najis al-ayn. The author of Al-Hidayah clarified with an example: the dog, according to the correct view, is not najis al-ayn, because it is permissible to keep a dog for guarding and hunting. Had the dog been najis al-ayn, benefiting from it would be prohibited. Since benefiting is permissible, it is not najis al-ayn, unlike swine which is najis al-ayn based on the text (innahu rijsun).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the principle that anything from which lawful benefit can be derived is not intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). The key example is the dog. While some schools consider the dog impure, the Hanafi school holds that it is not najis al-ayn because it is permissible to keep dogs for valid purposes like guarding and hunting. If it were substantially impure, such use would be impermissible. The exception is swine, which is declared najis al-ayn by explicit Quranic text. This principle is used in fiqh to determine the purity of animals and objects based on their permissibility of benefit.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Intrinsically impure; the substance itself is considered ritually impure regardless of contact with other impurities.
انتفاع (intifa'): Deriving benefit or utility, especially permissible use as allowed by Islamic law.
خنزير (khinzir): Swine, which is explicitly declared impure in the Quran (Surah al-An'am, verse 145).

The Necessity Principle: Water Becomes Musta'mal Upon Separation
Ruling with rational proof using legal maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when water becomes musta'mal and explain how the principle of necessity (darurah) supports this ruling.

Core Idea
Water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separation from the body — necessity only suspends the ruling during use, not after.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi legal principle (qa'ida) and its application in taharah: the timing of when water becomes musta'mal (used water). The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to justify the Hanafi position that collection is not required. We will walk through the reasoning step by step, from the maxim to its practical ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling being proved
What exactly is the Hanafi school ruling on here? It is the timing of when water acquires the status of musta'mal. The text asserts that water becomes musta'mal as soon as it separates from the body part being washed.
2
State the Hanafi position clearly
The Hanafi position is that water becomes musta'mal upon separation (infisal) from the limb, not upon collection in a vessel or container. This is the mufta bihi (relied upon) view.
This contrasts with some other schools.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof cited is the legal maxim: 'ما ثبت بالضرورة فهو يثبت بقدر الضرورة' (That which is established due to necessity is established only to the extent of necessity). This is a rational principle used to deduce the ruling.
The maxim is a qa'ida kulliya that governs many fiqh rules.
4
Explain how the principle applies
During the act of washing, water on the limb is not yet considered musta'mal out of necessity (because it must remain usable for the rest of the limb). But once the water separates, the necessity ceases, so the ruling of being musta'mal applies immediately.
Thus, separation is the end of necessity; collection is irrelevant.
5
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school requires that the used water be collected in a vessel before it becomes musta'mal. The Hanafis reject this condition, relying on the necessity principle.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences in how one handles water used for wudu'.

Source Text

That which is established due to necessity is established only to the extent of that necessity. Explanation: In this principle, the time when water becomes musta'mal is determined. The author of al-Hidayah cited this principle as evidence for determining when water becomes musta'mal. According to the Hanafis, water that separates from the body becomes musta'mal; it is not a condition that it collects in a vessel or place. The reasoning is: before separation, the ruling of being used is suspended due to necessity, but after separation there is no necessity, so it becomes musta'mal as soon as it separates.

This passage presents a legal principle (qa'ida) and applies it to the issue of musta'mal (used) water. The principle states that a ruling given due to necessity is limited to the extent of that necessity. The author of al-Hidayah uses this to prove the Hanafi position that water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separation from the limb, not upon collection. Before separation, the water is considered not yet used due to the necessity of washing; after separation, the necessity vanishes, so it becomes musta'mal. The text quotes supporting statements from al-Binayah, Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, and al-Hidayah itself, confirming this is the relied-upon view (al-sahih). The Hanafi school uniquely holds that collection is not required.

الْحَنَفِيَّة (al-Hanafiyyah): The Hanafi school of thought, followers of Imam Abu Hanifah.
الضَّرُورَة (al-darurah): Necessity that allows a temporary relaxation of standard rulings.
انْفِصَال (infisal): Separation of water from the limb being washed.

Key Takeaway
Water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separation from the body; this is deduced from the Hanafi legal maxim that a ruling granted due to necessity is limited to the extent of that necessity.

Dogs Are Not Inherently Impure: The Principle of Permitted Benefit
Proves dogs not najis al-'ayn via benefit principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that permitted benefit negates inherent impurity, and apply it to the case of the dog.

Core Idea
Lawful benefit proves purity: if Shari'ah allows using something, it cannot be najis al-'ayn.

Is a dog inherently impure (najis al-'ayn)? The Hanafi school answers with a clear principle: anything from which the Shari'ah permits benefit cannot be najis al-'ayn. This lesson unpacks that principle using the example of the dog, whose permitted uses for guarding and hunting show it is not inherently impure. We'll also contrast this with the pig, which is explicitly declared impure and may not be benefited from.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the opening statement: 'Indeed, whatever benefits are derived from it is not inherently impure.' This is a general legal maxim: permissibility of benefit negates inherent impurity.
The maxim is a type of istidlal (reasoning) based on the absence of evidence to the contrary.
2
Apply the principle to the dog
The dog is allowed for guarding and hunting – these are lawful benefits. Since the Shari'ah permits these uses, the dog cannot be najis al-'ayn.
If it were najis al-'ayn, benefiting from it would be prohibited (like the pig).
3
Contrast with the pig
The pig is explicitly declared impure (رجس) in the Quran and no benefit from it is allowed. Thus it remains najis al-'ayn.
The verse: 'فَإِنَّهُ رِجْسٌ' refers to the pig (as the nearest antecedent).
4
Note the evidence chain
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): if a thing were inherently impure, Shari'ah would prohibit all benefit from it. Since Shari'ah permits some benefit (guard, hunt, even sale/gift), the thing is not inherently impure.
This is a form of istihsan or logical inference supported by agreed-upon rulings.
5
Identify the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school disagrees – they hold dogs are najis al-'ayn despite permitted benefit. Understand the thamarah (practical effect): for Hanafis, a dog's saliva is pure; for Shafi'is, it is impure and requires washing.
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi and is considered 'al-asahh' (most sound) by the commentators.

Key Terms
نَجِس الْعَيْن (najis al-'ayn): Something whose physical substance is considered ritually impure, making it unlawful to touch or benefit from.
انْتِفَاع (intifa'): Lawful benefit or use permitted by the Shari'ah.

Key Takeaway

Tanning Achieved by Any Means That Removes Corruption
Refutes Imam Shafi'i's requirement of specific tanning agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of tanning and distinguish it from the Shafi'i requirement by applying the principle that the purpose of tanning is removing decay.

Core Idea
The purpose of tanning is preventing decay; any method achieving this suffices – restricting to specific agents is unnecessary.

This lesson examines a classic disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on what constitutes valid tanning (dibagh). The Hanafis take a practical approach: whatever removes bad odor and corruption from skin is tanning, whether sun, earth, or salt. Imam Shafi'i required specific vegetable substances. The dispute turns on a fundamental legal principle: when the objective is achieved, demanding additional conditions is meaningless. We will explore the Hanafi proof and its implication for daily purity rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on tanning
Identify the Hanafi definition: any substance or process that removes moisture and prevents decay – sun, earth, salt, or any other method.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Note that Imam Shafi'i restricts valid tanning to specific astringent agents like qaraz (acacia pods) and 'afs (oak gall).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'Anything that prevents the skin from decaying is tanning.' Also consider the rational argument: the purpose (removing corruption) is achieved by various means, so stipulating a particular agent is baseless.
The narration is from Kitab al-Athar, strengthening the Hanafi position.
4
Evaluate the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Understand the real-world consequence: Hanafis consider sun-dried or earth‑treated leather pure; Shafi'is require specific plant agents for purity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
The Hanafi position is relied upon in fatwa, supported by al-Hidayah and al-`Inayah, and consistent with the principle that means are defined by their ends.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

إِنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ إِذَا حَصَلَ بِشَيْءٍ فَلَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ غَيْرِهِ .
ترجمہ: جب مقصود کسی چیز سے حاصل ہو جائے تو پھر اس کے علاوہ کسی اور چیز کی شرط لگانے کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ کا مقصود اصول مذکورہ سے دباغت کا تعارف بیان کرنا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جو چیز کھال کی بدبو اور فساد کو دور کر دے وہ دباغت ہے، خواہ نمک کے ذریعے ہو ، یا دھوپ میں سکھا کر ہو، یا مٹی میں ڈال کر ہو، کیونکہ نجس رطوبتوں کو دور کرنے کی وجہ سے مقصود اس سے حاصل ہو جاتا ہے، لہذا اس کے علاوہ کسی اور چیز کی شرط لگانے کے کوئی معنی نہیں ہیں ، جیسا کہ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ نے درخت سلم ( ایک خاص قسم کا درخت ہے جس کے پتوں سے دباغت دی جاتی ہے ) اور مازو کے درخت کی شرط لگائی ہے۔
قَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ فِي كِتَابِ الْآثَارِ: أَخْبَرَنَا أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ عَنْ حَمَّادٍ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ قَالَ: كُلُّ شَيْءٍ يَمْنَعُ الْجِلْدَ مِنْ الْفَسَادِ فَهُوَ دِبَاعٌ) فَيَتَنَاوَلُ التَّشْمِيسَ وَالتُرِيبَ لأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ وَهُوَ مَنْعُ الْفَسَادِ بِإِزَالَةِ الرُّطُوبَاتِ النَّجِسَةِ يَحْصُلُ بِذَلِكَ فَلَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ غَيْرِهِ مِنْ قَرَطٍ أَوْ عَفْصٍ أَوْ شَكٍّ أَوْ نَحْوِهَا كَمَا شَرَطَهُ الشَّافِعِيُّ.
[العناية: كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذي يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۹۵، ط: دار الفکر] فَإِنَّ اسْمَ الدَّبَاغِ يَتَنَاوَلُ مَا يَقَعُ بِالتَّشْمِيسِ وَالتَّثْرِيبِ فَلا يُقَيَّدُ بِشَيْءٍ، وَلِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ يَحْصُلُ بِهِ فَلَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ غَيْرِهِ. البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارة، الطهارة بالدباغ، ج ۱ ص ۱۱۲، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
When the purpose is achieved by something, then stipulating something else is meaningless. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah intends to explain tanning. The principle is: anything that removes bad odor and corruption from skin is tanning, whether through salt, sun-drying, or burying in earth, because the purpose is achieved by removing the impure moisture. Therefore, stipulating something else is meaningless, as Imam Shafi'i stipulated the use of qarat and 'afs (specific tanning agents).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a legal principle and applies it to the definition of tanning (dibagh). The principle: if the intended objective is achieved by something, requiring something additional is unnecessary. The Hanafi school defines tanning as any process that prevents the skin from decaying by removing impure moisture. This includes sun-drying (tashmis), burying in earth (tatrib), or using salt. Imam Shafi'i, however, requires specific vegetable substances like qarat or 'afs. The Hanafi response is based on a narration from Imam Muhammad in Kitab al-Athar, reporting from Abu Hanifah from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'Anything that prevents the skin from decaying is tanning.' Since the purpose is achieved by various methods, stipulating a particular agent is baseless. The text cites al-`Inayah and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq to confirm this Hanafi stance.

Key Terms
دِبَاغ (dibagh): The process of treating animal skin to render it pure for use; Hanafis define it as any method preventing decay.
قَرَظ (qaraz): A specific tree (Acacia) whose pods are used for tanning; Shafi'is require it.
عَفْص ('afs): Oak gall, a substance used in tanning; Shafi'is include it as a required agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is considered valid tanning?
A) Only using qaraz (acacia) leaves
B) Only using 'afs (oak gall)
C) Drying the skin in the sun
D) Only using a combination of specific plants

Key Takeaway
Tanning is defined by its purpose – any method that prevents decay purifies the skin.

Principle 23: Achieving the Objective Suffices for Tanning
States a legal maxim and applies it to hide tanning methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of tanning, apply the maxim 'When the objective is achieved...' to justify the sufficiency of non-chemical methods, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i requirement of specific substances.

Core Idea
If a hide is purified from corruption, it is tanned — no specific substance is required.

This passage presents a foundational legal maxim and its application to hide tanning (dibagh). The Hanafi school, relying on the transmission of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, defines tanning broadly as any process that prevents corruption. This directly opposes the Shafi'i view that only specific astringent substances qualify. Understanding this maxim illuminates how Hanafi usul prioritizes the purpose (maqsud) over rigid conditions.

Guided Path
1
State the legal maxim
The passage opens with a qa'ida: 'When the objective is achieved by something, there is no meaning to stipulating something else.' This is the governing principle.
2
Identify the objective in tanning
The objective (maqsud) of tanning is to prevent corruption and remove impure moisture from the hide. This is explicitly mentioned in the commentary.
3
Locate the Hanafi proof
The dalil is the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i transmitted in Kitab al-Athar: 'Everything that prevents the hide from corruption is tanning.' This provides a broad definition.
The chain is: Muhammad → Abu Hanifa → Hammad → Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
4
Apply the maxim to the proof
Since sun and earth achieve the objective (preventing corruption) by removing moisture, they are valid tanning methods. Imposing extra conditions (e.g., specific tree barks) is meaningless once the purpose is fulfilled.
The phrase 'kullu shay'in' in the hadith includes all such methods.
5
Contrast with the opposing view
The Shafi'i school requires specific astringent substances (qarat, 'afs). The Hanafi response refutes this by appealing to the maxim: the objective is already achieved, so no additional stipulation is valid.
The practical difference: Hanafis allow hides tanned by sun/earth for prayer; Shafi'is do not.

(۲۳) إِنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ إِذَا حَصَلَ بِشَيْءٍ فَلَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ غَيْرِهِ . ترجمہ: جب مقصود کسی چیز سے حاصل ہو جائے تو پھر اس کے علاوہ کسی اور چیز کی شرط لگانے کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ کا مقصود اصول مذکورہ سے دباغت کا تعارف بیان کرنا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جو چیز کھال کی بدبو اور فساد کو دور کر دے وہ دباغت ہے، خواہ نمک کے ذریعے ہو ، یا دھوپ میں سکھا کر ہو، یا مٹی میں ڈال کر ہو، کیونکہ نجس رطوبتوں کو دور کرنے کی وجہ سے مقصود اس سے حاصل ہو جاتا ہے، لہذا اس کے علاوہ کسی اور چیز کی شرط لگانے کے کوئی معنی نہیں ہیں ، جیسا کہ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ نے درخت سلم ( ایک خاص قسم کا درخت ہے جس کے پتوں سے دباغت دی جاتی ہے ) اور مازو کے درخت کی شرط لگائی ہے۔ الهداية كتاب الطهارات، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۴۰، ط: رحمانیه قَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ فِي كِتَابِ الْآثَارِ: أَخْبَرَنَا أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ عَنْ حَمَّادٍ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ قَالَ: كُلُّ شَيْءٍ يَمْنَعُ الْجِلْدَ مِنْ الْفَسَادِ فَهُوَ دِبَاعٌ) فَيَتَنَاوَلُ التَّشْمِيسَ وَالتُرِيبَ لأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ وَهُوَ مَنْعُ الْفَسَادِ بِإِزَالَةِ الرُّطُوبَاتِ النَّحِسَةِ يَحْصُلُ بِذَلِكَ فَلَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ غَيْرِهِ مِنْ قَرَطٍ أَوْ عَفْصٍ أَوْ شَكٍّ أَوْ نَحْوِهَا كَمَا شَرَطَهُ الشَّافِعِيُّ. العناية: كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذي يجوز به الوضوء، ج ۱ ص ۹۵، ط: دار الفکر فَإِنَّ اسْمَ الدَّبَاغِ يَتَنَاوَلُ مَا يَقَعُ بِالشَّشْمِيسِ وَالتَّتُرِيبِ فَلا يُقَيَّدُ بِشَيْءٍ، وَلِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ يَحْصُلُ بِهِ فَلَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ غَيْرِهِ. البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارة، الطهارة بالدباغ، ج ۱ ص ۱۱۲، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
(23) When the objective is achieved by something, there is no meaning to stipulating something else. Translation: When the objective is obtained from something, then there is no benefit in stipulating something else. Explanation: The author of Hidayah intends from this principle to explain the definition of tanning, the conclusion of which is that whatever removes the bad smell and corruption of the hide is tanning, whether by salt, or drying in the sun, or burying in earth, because due to removing impure moisture, the objective is achieved, so there is no meaning to stipulating something else, as Imam al-Shafi'i stipulated the tree of Salam and oak tree. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Taharah, Bab al-Ma' alladhi yajuz bihi al-wudu', vol. 1, p. 40, Rahmaniyyah ed. Muhammad said in Kitab al-Athar: Abu Hanifa informed us from Hammad from Ibrahim that he said: Everything that prevents the hide from corruption is tanning. So it includes sun-drying and burying in earth because the objective – preventing corruption by removing impure moisture – is achieved by that, so there is no meaning to stipulating something else like qarat, 'afs, shakk, or similar, as al-Shafi'i stipulated. Al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Taharah, Bab al-Ma' alladhi yajuz bihi al-wudu', vol. 1, p. 95, Dar al-Fikr ed. Indeed, the term tanning includes what occurs by sun-drying and burying in earth, so it is not restricted to something, and because the objective is achieved by it, so there is no meaning to stipulating something else. Al-Bahr al-Raiq, Kitab al-Taharah, al-Taharah bi al-dabagh, vol. 1, p. 112, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami ed.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the definition of hide tanning (dabagh). It opens with a legal maxim (qa'idah): 'When the objective is achieved by something, there is no meaning to stipulating something else.' The author applies this to tanning: any method that removes the hide's bad smell and corruption—whether salt, sun-drying, or burying in earth—constitutes valid tanning. The proof is a narration from Imam Muhammad's Kitab al-Athar, tracing back through Abu Hanifa, Hammad, and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, who states that anything which prevents hide from corruption is tanning. This includes sun and earth because they achieve the objective by removing impure moisture. The passage then explicitly refutes Imam al-Shafi'i, who required specific materials like qarat (a type of oak gall) or 'afs (oak apple). The Hanafi position relies on the broader definition and the sufficiency of achieving the purpose. Later quotes from Al-'Inayah and Al-Bahr al-Raiq reinforce this, emphasizing that the term 'dabagh' is not restricted to any particular substance.

Key Terms
دباغة (dibāghah): The process of removing bad smell and corruption from a hide, making it pure.
مقصود (maqsūd): The intended purpose or objective of a legal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must use specific oak galls called qarat.

C) It must remove bad odor and corruption from the hide.
D) It must involve soaking in saltwater.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the purpose (maqsud) of a legal act overrides rigid conditions: any method that achieves the objective of purifying a hide is valid tanning.

Slaughter Purifies Hide and Meat of Non-Edible Animals
States and proves that slaughter purifies hide and meat of non-edible animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that valid slaughter purifies both hide and meat of non-edible animals, apply the rational proof (qiyas with tanning), and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether meat is included.

Core Idea
Valid slaughter purifies the whole animal—like tanning, but more powerful—because it prevents impure moisture from ever settling.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi principle: any animal whose hide can be purified by tanning is also purified by ritual slaughter (dhabh). The ruling covers not just the hide but also the meat, even for animals whose meat is unlawful to eat. The proof hinges on a rational analogy: since tanning removes impurity after it adheres, slaughter, which prevents impurity from adhering in the first place, is even more effective. We will also explore a minority Hanafi view that limits purification to the hide alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
State the core ruling: the hide of any animal that can be tanned becomes pure by valid slaughter (dhabh) performed by an eligible person (Muslim or Kitabi) with tasmiyah. Note that the slaughter of a Zoroastrian does not purify.
The condition of eligibility (ahl al-dhabh) is essential.
2
Understand the proof by qiyas (rational analogy)
The text argues that slaughter acts like tanning by removing impure moisture. Tanning removes moisture after it has already adhered; slaughter prevents moisture from adhering altogether. Therefore, slaughter is a fortiori (اولى) a purifying act.
The proof is a dalil 'aqli (rational proof) based on the principle that prevention is more effective than removal.
3
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (mashayikh of Balkh) hold that only the hide is purified, not the meat and fat. The majority position, preferred by Al-Hidayah and Bada'i' al-Sana'i', holds that all parts are purified because impurity flows from blood, which is removed by slaughter.
The disagreement is about the scope (hide only vs. entire animal), not the principle itself.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete case
If one prays while carrying meat from a slaughtered fox (a non-edible animal) more than the size of a dirham, the prayer is valid because the meat is pure. This is a practical test of the ruling.
The validity of prayer is used as evidence for purity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah and the preferred view in later Hanafi works, slaughter purifies all parts of the animal. This is the position relied upon for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on the removal of blood (مَحَلُّ النَّجَاسَةِ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

کی کھال دباغت سے پاک ہو جاتی ہے اسی طرح ذبح کرنے سے بھی پاک ہو جاتی ہے، لیکن شرط یہ ہے کہ یہ ذبح کرنا ایسے شخص سے ہو جو ذبح کا اہل ہو، چنانچہ مجوسی کا ذبح کرنا اس کو پاک نہیں کرے گا، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے مُطَهِّرَةً، وَذَكَرَ الضَّمِيرَ فِي الأَنَّهُ لأَنَّ الزَّكَاةَ بِمَعْنَى الذَّبْحِ، وَإِنَّمَا تَعْمَلُ عَمَلَ الدَّبَاغِ فِي إِزَالَةِ الرُّطُوبَاتِ النَّحِسَةِ لِأَنَّهُ يَمْنَعُ مِنْ الْصَالِهَا وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَكَذَلِكَ يَطْهُرُ لَحْمُهُ) أَيْ لَحْمُ مَا ذُبِحَ حَتَّى إِذَا صَلَّى وَمَعَهُ مِنْ لَحْمِ التَّعْلَبِ الْمَذْبُوحِ أَوْ نَحْوِهِ أَكْثَرُ مِنْ قَدْرِ الدِّرْهَمِ جَازَتْ صَلَاتُهُ. العناية: كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء ، ج ا ص ۹۵، ط: دار الكتب العلمية ] كُلُّ حَيَوَان يَطْهُرُ بِالدِّبَاعَ يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالزَّكَاةِ، فَهَذَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَطْهُرُ لَحْمُهُ وَشَحْمُهُ وَسَائِرُ أَجْزَائِهِ؛ لأنَّ الْحَيَوَانَ اسْمٌ لِجُمُلَةِ الْأَجْزَاءِ. وَقَالَ بَعْضُ مَشَايِخِنَا وَمَشَايِخِ بَلْخِ : إِنَّ كُلَّ حَيَوَانِ يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالدِّبَاغِ يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالزَّكَاةِ فَأَمَّا اللَّحْمُ وَالشَّحُمُ وَنَحْوَهُمَا فَلا يَطْهُرُ، وَالأوَّلُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ أَنَّ النَّجَاسَهُ لِمَكَانِ الدَّمِ الْمَسْفُوحٍ، وَقَدْ زَالَ بِالزَّكَاةِ. بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الطهارة، فصل بيان ما يقع به التطهير، ج ۱ ص ۸۶، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
(24) Whatever hide is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter. Translation: The hide of an animal that becomes pure by tanning also becomes pure by slaughtering. Explanation: The conclusion of this principle is that just as the hide of an animal becomes pure by tanning, similarly it becomes pure by slaughtering, but on condition that the slaughter is done by a person who is eligible for slaughter; thus the slaughter of a Zoroastrian does not purify it. The reason is that slaughtering performs the function of tanning in that it removes impure moisture. Similarly, slaughtering also purifies the meat of the animal, even if it is an animal whose meat is not eaten; that is, by slaughtering, the meat of non-edible animals also becomes pure. This is why if one slaughters a non-edible animal and carries its meat in his pocket while praying, his prayer is valid. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Taharah, Bab al-Ma' alladhi yajuz bihi al-wudu', vol. 1, p. 40, Rahmaniyyah ed. Then: 'Whatever hide is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter (zakah)'. He means the slaughter performed by an eligible person with the tasmiyah, for the slaughter of a Zoroastrian is not purifying. He mentioned the pronoun in 'annahu' because 'zakah' means 'dhabh' (slaughter). It performs the function of tanning in removing impure moisture because it prevents it from adhering. Tanning removes after adherence, and when tanning after adherence is both removive and purifying, then slaughter, which prevents adherence, is more deserving to be purifying. And his statement: 'Similarly its meat is purified' means the meat of what is slaughtered so that if one prays with him having meat of a slaughtered fox or the like more than the size of a dirham, his prayer is valid. Al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Taharah, Bab al-Ma' alladhi yajuz bihi al-wudu', vol. 1, p. 95, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah ed. 'Every animal whose hide is purified by tanning, its hide is purified by slaughter; this indicates that its meat, fat, and all its parts are purified, because 'animal' is a name for the entirety of parts.' And some of our mashayikh and the mashayikh of Balkh said: 'Every animal whose hide is purified by tanning, its hide is purified by slaughter; as for meat, fat, and similar, they are not purified.' The first is closer to correctness, because the impurity is due to the flowing blood, and it has been removed by slaughter. Bada'i' al-Sana'i', Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl bayan ma yaqa' bihi al-tathir, vol. 1, p. 86, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah ed.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. The ruling states that any animal hide that can be purified by tanning is also purified by valid slaughter (dhabh) performed by an eligible person (Muslim or kitabi) with tasmiyah. Slaughter works like tanning by preventing impure moisture from settling, and since it acts before putrefaction, it is even more effective than tanning. The commentary then addresses the meat: slaughter also purifies the meat of all parts of the animal, even if the animal is not edible (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm). Proof: if one carries such meat in prayer, the prayer is valid. Then a dispute is noted: some Hanafi scholars (from Balkh) hold that only the hide is purified, not the meat and fat. However, the author (following Bada'i' al-Sana'i') prefers the first opinion because impurity originates from flowing blood, which is removed by slaughter, thus all parts become pure. The relied-upon position is that both hide and meat of non-edible animals are purified by slaughter.

Key Terms
ذكاة (dhakāh): Ritual slaughter (dhabh) performed by an eligible person with tasmiyah.
غير ماكول اللحم (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm): An animal whose meat is not lawful to eat (e.g., fox, lion).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fox is slaughtered according to Islamic law, which parts become pure according to the preferred Hanafi view?
A) Only the hide
B) Only the meat
C) The hide, meat, and all parts
D) None; non-edible animals cannot be purified by slaughter

Key Takeaway
Valid slaughter purifies both hide and meat of any animal that can be tanned, based on the rational analogy with tanning and the removal of blood.

Slaughter Purifies Hide Like Tanning
Establishes slaughter as equivalent to tanning in purifying hide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that slaughter purifies the hide of any animal just as tanning does, identify the condition for slaughter to be purifying, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding meat and fat.

Core Idea
Slaughter purifies hide as tanning does; impurity is blood, removed by slaughter.

This lesson draws from Al-Hidayah's ruling on the equivalence of slaughter (dhakah) and tanning (dibagh) in purifying hides. The author uses a powerful analogy to extend the ruling to meat and fat. You will examine the reasoning (ta'lil) and an intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its scope
The text opens with a general rule: whatever animal's hide is purified by tanning is also purified by proper slaughter. Note that all animals whose hide can be tanned (excluding pigs and dogs according to Hanafis) are included.
The principle is conditional on slaughter being valid (Muslim with tasmiyah).
2
Understand the analogy (qiyas jaliy)
Tanning purifies hide by removing impure moisture after it has seeped in. Slaughter prevents the moisture from seeping in altogether. Since prevention is stronger than removal, slaughter is more effective in purification.
This is an a fortiori argument (awlā).
3
Extend the ruling to meat and fat
The text argues that because 'animal' refers to all parts, the meat and fat are also purified. The analogy extends from hide to the whole body.
The extension is supported by the practical test: prayer with a piece of slaughtered fox meat more than a dirham's weight is valid.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars (mashayikh of Balkh) restrict purification to the hide only. They do not consider meat and fat purified. The preferred view (al-awwal aqrab) is that all parts are purified because impurity is from spilled blood, and slaughter removes it.
The thamarah (practical difference) affects validity of prayer with meat on one's person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مَا يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالدِّبَاغِ يَطْهُرُ بِالذَّكَاةِ
ثُمَّ مَا يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالدِّبَاغِ يَطْهُرُ بِالزَّكَاةِ) يَعْنِي الزَّكَاةَ الْحَاصِلَةَ مِنْ الْأَهْلِ بِالتَّسْمِيَةِ، فَإِنَّ دَكَاةَ الْمَجُوسِيِّ لَيْسَتْ مُطَهِّرَةً، وَذَكَرَ الضَّمِيرَ فِي الأَنَّهُ لأَنَّ الزَّكَاةَ بِمَعْنَى الذَّبْحِ، وَإِنَّمَا تَعْمَلُ عَمَلَ الدَّبَاغِ فِي إِزَالَةِ الرُّطُوبَاتِ النَّجِسَةِ لِأَنَّهُ يَمْنَعُ مِنْ الْصَالِهَا بِهِ، وَالدِّبَاغُ مُزِيلٌ بَعْدَ الاتِّصَالِ، وَلَمَّا كَانَ الدِّبَاغُ بَعْدَ الاتِّصَالِ مُزِيلًا وَمُطَهْرًا كَانَتْ الذَّكَاةُ الْمَانِعَةُ مِنْ الاتِّصَالِ أَوْلَى أَنْ تَكُونَ مُطَهِّرَةً . وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَكَذَلِكَ يَطْهُرُ لَحْمُهُ) أَيْ لَحْمُ مَا ذُبِحَ حَتَّى إِذَا صَلَّى وَمَعَهُ مِنْ لَحْمِ التَّعْلَبِ الْمَذْبُوحِ أَوْ نَحْوِهِ أَكْثَرُ مِنْ قَدْرِ الدِّرْهَمِ جَازَتْ صَلَاتُهُ.
كُلُّ حَيَوَان يَطْهُرُ بِالدِّبَاغِ يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالزَّكَاةِ، فَهَذَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَطْهُرُ لَحْمُهُ وَشَحْمُهُ وَسَائِرُ أَجْزَائِهِ؛ لأنَّ الْحَيَوَانَ اسْمٌ لِجُمُلَةِ الْأَجْزَاءِ.
وَقَالَ بَعْضُ مَشَايِخِنَا وَمَشَايِخِ بَلْخِ : إِنَّ كُلَّ حَيَوَانِ يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالدِّبَاغِ يَطْهُرُ جِلْدُهُ بِالزَّكَاةِ فَأَمَّا اللَّحْمُ وَالشَّحُمُ وَنَحْوَهُمَا فَلا يَطْهُرُ، وَالأوَّلُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ أَنَّ النَّجَاسَةَ لِمَكَانِ الدَّمِ الْمَسْفُوحٍ، وَقَدْ زَالَ بِالزَّكَاةِ.
Indeed, whatever its hide is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter. Then, whatever its hide is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter (zakah) meaning the slaughter obtained from an Ahl (Muslim) with the name (of Allah). For the slaughter of a Magian is not purifying. And he mentioned the pronoun in 'annahu because zakah is in the sense of slaughter. And it only performs the action of tanning in removing impure moistures because it prevents their seepage. And tanning is a remover after contact. And since tanning after contact is a remover and purifier, slaughter which prevents contact is more entitled to be a purifier. And His statement: 'And likewise its meat is purified' means the meat of what is slaughtered so that if one prays and with him is meat of slaughtered fox or the like more than a dirham's weight, his prayer is valid. Every animal whose hide is purified by tanning, its hide is purified by slaughter. This indicates that its meat, fat, and all its parts are purified because 'animal' is a name for the totality of parts. And some of our mashayikh and mashayikh of Balkh said: Every animal whose hide is purified by tanning, its hide is purified by slaughter. As for the meat and fat and the like, they are not purified. And the first is closer to correctness because the impurity is due to the spilled blood, and it has been removed by slaughter.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi legal principle (asl) that the hide of any animal is purified by proper slaughter just as by tanning. The condition is that slaughter must be by a Muslim with tasmiyah (saying Bismillah). The reasoning (ta'lil) is that slaughter prevents impure moisture from seeping into the hide, while tanning removes it after contact—so slaughter is more effective. The principle extends to the meat: even non-edible animals like fox, the meat becomes pure, as indicated by the validity of prayer with such meat less than a dirham's weight. An intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded: some scholars from Balkh restrict purification to the hide only. The preferred view (al-awwal aqrab) is that all parts are purified because impurity is due to blood, which is removed by slaughter.

Key Terms
ذكاة (dhakah): Slaughter of an animal according to Islamic law, including the conditions of being by a Muslim with tasmiyah.
دباغ (dibagh): Tanning the hide, which removes moisture and purifies it.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Pronouncing the name of Allah (Bismillah) at the time of slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The hide must be tanned first.
D) The animal must be alive before slaughter.

Key Takeaway
Slaughter (dhakah) by a Muslim with tasmiyah purifies the hide and meat of any animal, by analogy to tanning, with the impure blood removed.

Purity of Hair and Bones of Dead Animals
Rational proof for purity of dead animal parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hair, bones, and similar parts of a dead animal, explain the rational proof based on the presence or absence of life, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Death nullifies life; what never had life remains pure.

The Hanafi school holds a distinctive position on the purity of certain parts of a dead animal. While the flesh becomes impure because it once had life, parts like hair, bones, and sinews never had life themselves—they do not feel pain when cut. This lesson unpacks the rational proof (dalil aqli) that underlies this ruling and explains why Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, leading to a practical difference in rulings on wudu water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Locate the principle stated at the beginning: 'Indeed, a thing when there is no life in it, death does not affect it.' This is the foundation of the Hanafi argument.
The principle is both linguistic and rational: death is defined as the cessation of life, so it can only affect that which previously had life.
2
Apply the principle to the ruling
The hair, bone, sinew, and similar parts are ruled pure because they never contained life. The text gives evidence: cutting a cow's horn or shearing wool does not cause the animal pain, showing these parts lack life.
Pain is used as an empirical indicator of life (hayat). If no pain, no life in that part.
3
Present the dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that all parts of a dead body are impure, because the entire body becomes impure at death. The Hanafi response is to reject the premise—only parts that had life become impure.
The dispute is not about the rule but about the definition of 'dead body' (maytah) and its constituent parts.
4
Trace the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is a syllogism: (1) Death only changes what it can reach—life. (2) Hair, bones, etc., have no life. (3) Therefore death does not change them; they remain pure. The text emphasizes this with multiple examples (horn, hoof, wool, etc.).
This is a qiyas form: the asl is something that has life (flesh), the far is what never had life, the illa is the presence of life.
5
Note the Quranic support
The passage cites Quran 16:80 where Allah mentions wool, fur, and hair as blessings without restricting them to live animals. This supports the idea that such items are permissible (and pure) even from dead animals.
The Quranic verse is used as a corroborating dalil (mu'ayyid), not the primary proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الشَّيْءَ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنُ فِيهِ حَيَاةٌ لَا يَحِلُّهُ الْمَوْتُ .
(وَشَعْرُ الْمَيْتَةِ وَعَظْمُهَا وَعَصَبُهَا طَاهِبٌ) ذَكَرَهُ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ إِذَا وَقَعَ فِي الْمَاءِ هَلْ يَجُوزُ الْوُضُوءُ بِهِ أَوْ لَا؟ عِنْدَنَا يَجُوزُ بِهِ الْوُضُوء لِكَوْنِهَا طَاهِرَةً. وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ نَجِسٌ لِأَنَّهُ) أَيْ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْمَيِّتِ وَالْمَيِّتُ نَجِسٌ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ. قُلْنَا: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ كُلَّ جُزْءٍ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْمَيِّتِ نَجِسٌ بَلُ النَّجِسُ مِنْهُ مَا كَانَ فِيهِ حَيَاةٌ زَالَتْ بِالْمَوْتِ وَهَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءُ لَا حَيَاةَ فِيهَا حَتَّى لَا يَتَأَلَّمَ بِقَطْعِهَا الْحَيَوَانُ، فَإِنَّ قَطعَ قَرْنِ الْبَقَرَةِ لَا يُؤْلِمُهَا وَجَزُّ صُوفِ الْغَنَمِ كَذَلِكَ فَلا يُحِلُّهَا الْمَوْتُ إِذِ الْمَوْتُ زَوَالُ الْحَيَاةِ.
(وَشَعُرُ الْمَيْتَةِ وَعَظْمُهَا طَاهِرٌ لأَنَّ الْحَيَاةَ لَا تُحِلُّهُمَا حَتَّى لَا تَتَأَلَّمَ بِقَطْعِهِمَا فَلَا يَحِلُّهُمَا الْمَوْتُ وَهُوَ الْمُنَجِّسُ ، وَكَذَلِكَ الْعَصَبُ وَالْحَافِرُ وَالْحُقُ وَالظُّلُفُ وَالْقَرْنُ وَالصُّوفَ وَالْوَبَرُ وَالرِّيشُ وَالسِّنُّ وَالْمِنْقَارُ وَالْمِخْلَبُ لِمَا ذَكَرُنَا، وَلِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: (وَمِنْ أَصْوَافِهَا وَأَوْبَارِهَا وَأَشْعَارِهَا) امْتَنَّ بِهَا عَلَيْنَا مِنْ غَيْرِ فَصْلٍ.
Indeed, a thing when there is no life in it, death does not affect it. And the hair of a dead animal, its bone, and its sinew are pure. He mentioned it considering that if it falls into water, can one perform wudu with it or not? According to us, one can perform wudu with it because they are pure. Al-Shafi'i said: Impure, because each of them is from the parts of the dead body, and the dead body is impure in all its parts. We say: We do not concede that every part of the dead body is impure. Rather, what is impure from it is that in which there was life that ceased by death. And these things have no life in them, so much so that the animal does not feel pain by cutting them. For cutting the horn of a cow does not pain it, and shearing the wool of a sheep likewise. So death does not affect them because death is the cessation of life. And the hair of a dead animal and its bone are pure because life does not affect them so that they do not feel pain by cutting them, so death does not affect them, and it is what makes impure. And likewise the sinew, hoof, shank, cloven hoof, horn, wool, fur, feathers, tooth, beak, and talon, due to what we mentioned. And due to His statement: 'And from their wool, fur, and hair' He bestowed them upon us without distinction (between dead and alive).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (dalil aqli) for the Hanafi ruling that hair, bones, and similar parts of a dead animal (maytah) are pure (tahir). The principle (asl) is that death only affects what once had life. Since hair, bone, sinew, etc. never had life—evidenced by the fact that cutting them does not cause the animal pain—they are not affected by death and thus remain pure. The text contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's view that all parts of a dead animal are impure. The Hanafi response (qulna) distinguishes between parts that had life (like flesh) and parts that never had life. The passage then lists many parts (horn, fur, wool, feathers, etc.) and supports the ruling with the Quranic verse (16:80) where Allah mentions these items as blessings without restricting them to live animals, implying they can be used even from dead ones.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal, whether died naturally or improperly slaughtered; its flesh is impure but not all parts.
طاهر (tahir): Legally pure; not considered impure, can be used in acts of worship like wudu.
حياة (hayat): Life; the presence of life in a part of the animal is the criterion for whether death makes it impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the Quran explicitly declares them pure.
B) Because they are from a slaughtered animal, not a dead one.
C) Because they never had life, as evidenced by the lack of pain when cut.
D) Because they are dry and dry items cannot become impure.

Key Takeaway
Death only renders impure what once had life; hair, bones, and similar parts that never had life remain pure.

8 min
Purity of Non-Living Parts of a Dead Animal: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling with proof and rebuttal of Shafi'i on purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the purity of hair, bones, and sinews of a dead animal, and explain the key rational and textual proofs used to rebut the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Only parts that had life become impure upon death; lifeless parts remain pure.

In this passage, the Hanafi school argues that certain parts of a dead animal—such as hair, bones, and sinews—are pure (tahir), even though the animal itself is considered carrion (maytah) and its meat is haram. The proof rests on a rational principle: impurity from death only attaches to parts that housed life and lost it. Since these parts never had life (as evidenced by the lack of pain when cut), death does not affect them. This lesson unpacks the argument and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view that the entire carcass is impure.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: hair, bones, sinews, and similar parts of a dead animal are pure (tahir).
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that these same parts are impure (najis) because they are part of the dead body.
The entire carcass is considered impure in the Shafi'i school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: the dead body is impure by consensus; all its parts share that ruling.
This is a general principle applied to every component.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: not every part is impure—only those that had life and lost it. Hair, bones, etc., never had life (proven by absence of pain when cut), so death does not permeate them.
A rational (aqli) proof is used, supported by the Quranic verse 16:80.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: if such a part falls into water, Hanafis consider the water pure for wudu; Shafi'is consider it impure.
This affects daily practice, especially for ablution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۵) إِنَّ الشَّيْءَ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنُ فِيهِ حَيَاةٌ لَا يَحِلُّهُ الْمَوْتُ .
ترجمہ: جب کسی چیز کے اندرحیات نہ ہو تو اس میں موت سرایت نہیں کرے گی۔ تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ اصول مذکورہ سے اس بات کو ثابت کرنا چاہتے ہیں کہ مردار کے بال اور ہڑی پاک ہیں یعنی اگر مراد کے بال یا ہڈی وغیرہ پانی کے اندر گر جائے تو اس پانی سے وضو کرنا جائز ہے، اسی بات کو ثابت کرنے کے لئے موصوف نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل عقلی کے پیش کیا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مردار کے تمام اجزاء نا پاک نہیں ہوتے بلکہ صرف وہ اجزاء ناپاک ہیں جن میں حیات ہو اور موت کی وجہ سے وہ حیات زائل ہو گئی، اور بال اور ہڈی وغیرہ ان چیزوں میں حیات نہیں ہوتی ، کیونکہ ان میں سے اگر کسی چیز کو کاٹا جائے تو اس سے جانور کو تکلیف محسوس نہیں ہوتی ، پس جب ان میں حیات نہیں اور اصول ذکر کیا جا چکا ہے کہ جس چیز کے اندرحیات نہ ہو تو موت اس میں حلول نہیں کرتی ، لہذا ان چیزوں میں بھی موت حلول نہیں کرے گی، کیونکہ موت تو حیات کے زائل ہونے کا نام ہے اور وہ یہاں پر مفقود ہے۔ جب ان میں حیات نہیں ہے تو پھر ان کے مرنے یا مردار ہونے کا کوئی سوال ہی نہیں پیدا ہوتا، جب یہ مردار نہیں ہیں تو نا پاک بھی نہیں ہوں گے۔
الهداية كتاب الطهارة، باب الماء الذى يجوز به الوضوء، ج ا ص ۴۰، ط: رحمانیه (وَشَعْرُ الْمَيْتَةِ وَعَظْمُهَا وَعَصَبُهَا طَاهِبٌ) ذَكَرَهُ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ إِذَا وَقَعَ فِي الْمَاءِ هَلْ يَجُوزُ الْوُضُوءُ بِهِ أَوْ لَا؟ عِنْدَنَا يَجُوزُ بِهِ الْوُضُوء لِكَوْنِهَا طَاهِرَةً. وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ نَجِسٌ لِأَنَّهُ) أَيْ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْمَيِّتِ وَالْمَيِّتُ نَجِسٌ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ. قُلْنَا: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ كُلَّ جُزْءٍ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْمَيِّتِ نَجِسٌ بَلُ النَّجِسُ مِنْهُ مَا كَانَ فِيهِ حَيَاةٌ زَالَتْ بِالْمَوْتِ وَهَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءُ لَا حَيَاةَ فِيهَا حَتَّى لَا يَتَأَلَّمَ بِقَطْعِهَا الْحَيَوَانُ، فَإِنَّ قَطعَ قَرْنِ الْبَقَرَةِ لَا يُؤْلِمُهَا وَجَزُّ صُوفِ الْغَنَمِ كَذَلِكَ فَلا يُحِلُّهَا الْمَوْتُ إِذِ الْمَوْتُ زَوَالُ الْحَيَاةِ.
العناية كتاب الطهارة، ج ۱ ص ۹۶، ط: دار الفكر] (وَشَعُرُ الْمَيْتَةِ وَعَظْمُهَا طَاهِرٌ لأَنَّ الْحَيَدَةَ لَا تُحِلُّهُمَا حَتَّى لَا تَتَأَلَّمَ بِقَطْعِهِمَا فَلَا يُحِلُّهُمَا الْمَوْتُ وَهُوَ الْمُنَجِّسُ ، وَكَذَلِكَ الْعَصَبُ وَالْحَافِرُ وَالْحُقُ وَالظُّلُفُ وَالْقَرْنُ وَالصُّوفَ وَالْوَبَرُ وَالرِّيشُ وَالسِّنُّ وَالْمِنْقَارُ وَالْمِخْلَبُ لِمَا ذَكَرُنَا، وَلِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: (وَمِنْ أَصْوَافِهَا وَأَوْبَارِهَا وَأَشْعَارِهَا) امْتَنَّ بِهَا عَلَيْنَا مِنْ غَيْرِ فَصْلٍ.
الاختيار لتعليل المختار كتاب الطهارة، ج ا ص ١٦ ، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
(25) Indeed, when a thing has no life in it, death does not permeate it. Translation: When a thing has no life, death will not penetrate it. Explanation: The author of Al-Hidayah wants to prove by this mentioned principle that the hair and bones of a dead animal are pure, meaning that if the hair or bone of a dead animal falls into water, then performing ablution with that water is permissible. To prove this, the mentioned person presented this principle as a rational proof. The gist of which is that not all parts of a dead animal are impure; rather, only those parts are impure in which there was life and that life was removed by death. And hair, bones, etc. have no life in them, because if one of them is cut, the animal does not feel pain. Thus, when there is no life in them, and the mentioned principle has already been stated that if a thing has no life, death does not permeate it, therefore death will not permeate these things either, because death is the name for removal of life and that is absent here. When there is no life in them, then no question of their dying or being carrion arises. When they are not carrion, they will not be impure. Al-Hidayah, Book of Purity, Chapter on Water Permissible for Ablution (and the hair of a dead animal and its bone and its sinew are pure) He mentioned it with consideration that if it falls into water, is ablution permissible with it or not? With us, ablution is permissible because they are pure. And al-Shafi'i said: it is impure because each one of them is from among the parts of the dead body and the dead body is impure with all its parts. We said: we do not accept that every part of the dead body is impure. Rather, the impure from it is that which had life that was removed by death. And these things have no life in them, such that the animal does not feel pain at their cutting. For the cutting of the horn of a cow does not pain it, and shearing the wool of sheep likewise. So death does not permeate them, for death is the removal of life. Al-Inayah, Book of Purity (and the hair of a dead animal and its bone are pure because life does not permeate them such that they do not feel pain at their cutting; thus death does not permeate them, and it is the impurifier. And similarly the sinew, the hoof, the fetlock, the cloven hoof, the horn, the wool, the camel hair, the feathers, the tooth, the beak, and the talon, for what we have mentioned, and because of His statement, the Most High: 'And from their wool and their fur and their hair' He bestows favors upon us without distinction.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the purity of parts of a dead animal (maytah) that never contained life, such as hair, bones, and sinews. The Hanafi position, as stated in Al-Hidayah and Al-Inayah, is that these parts are pure (tahir). The Shafi'i position is that they are impure because they are parts of a dead body, which is entirely impure. The Hanafi rebuttal is based on a rational principle (dalil aqli) that impurity from death only attaches to parts that had life and lost it. Since hair, bones, sinews, horns, hooves, wool, etc. have no life in them (as evidenced by the absence of pain when cut), death cannot permeate them, and thus they remain pure. The proof includes the Quranic verse (16:80) where Allah mentions these animal products as favours without distinguishing between living and dead animals, implying their purity. The Hanafi ruling is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the school.

Key Terms
ميته (maytah): A dead animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter.
حياة (hayat): Life; the presence of which in a body part makes it subject to impurity upon death.
نجس (najis): Impure; legally filthy and preventing the validity of prayer if present on body or clothes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why are the hair and bones of a dead animal considered pure (tahir)?
A) Because they can be washed and purified
B) Because they never had life, so death does not affect them
C) Because the animal was not slaughtered Islamically
D) Because all parts of a dead body are pure by default

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, impurity from death attaches only to parts that housed life; lifeless parts like hair, bones, and horns remain pure.
