Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Well Issues Based on Narrations, Not Analogy
Statement of a methodological principle for well purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that well purification rulings are based on transmitted narrations (athar) rather than analogical reasoning (qiyas), and explain why qiyas was abandoned.

Core Idea
Well purification rules follow narrations, not analogy, because analogy yields two opposite conclusions.

In Hanafi fiqh, the rulings concerning the purification of wells after contamination are unique: they are not derived through analogical reasoning (qiyas) but are firmly based on transmitted narrations (athar) from the Prophet ﷺ and the Companions. This principle is a cornerstone of the chapter on purity (taharah) and demonstrates that in certain cases, transmitted evidence overrides rational deduction. Understanding this principle helps you appreciate why the Hanafi school prescribes specific bucket counts for different animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the opening statement: 'مسائل الآبار مبنية على اتباع الآثار دون القياس'. Understand that this is a methodological rule for well purification.
2
Understand the two conflicting analogies
Analogy (qiyas) on well water leads to two contradictory results: (1) it never becomes impure because water continuously springs up, like flowing water; (2) it never becomes pure because all parts (walls, sludge) become contaminated. Both are possible, so qiyas is inconclusive.
One qiyas uses the illa of continuous renewal; the other uses the illa of complete mixing.
3
See the Hanafi departure from qiyas
Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf agreed that the water is like flowing water in principle, but they left that qiyas and followed the narrations (athar). The consensus of the Salaf supports that removing a specified number of buckets purifies the well without needing to wash walls or remove sludge.
This is an example of ترك القياس للأثر (abandoning analogy for narration).
4
Recognize the practical outcome
The well becomes pure simply by removing a certain number of buckets based on the type of animal that fell in. No qiyas is used; the numbers are taken directly from transmitted reports.
For example, a small animal like a mouse may require 20 buckets, a large animal like a dog may require 40 or the entire well emptied, as per narrations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢٦) إِنَّ مَسَائِلَ الْآبَارِ مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى اتِّبَاعِ الْآثَارِ دُونَ الْقِيَاسِ .
ترجمہ: کنویں کے مسائل اتباع آثار پر مبنی ہیں نہ کہ قیاس پر۔
تشریح: اصول کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ کنویں کے تمام مسائل التباع آثار پر بنی ہیں ، اس میں قیاس کا کوئی دخل نہیں ہے، قیاس اس باب میں اس لئے معتبر نہیں کہ کنویں کے پانی میں دو قیاس متضاد ہیں۔ ایک قیاس کا تقاضہ تو یہ ہے کہ پانی ناپاک ہی نہ ہو اس لئے کہ کنویں میں نیچے سے مسلسل پانی نکلتا رہتا ہے، لہذا کنویں کا پانی آپ جاری کے حکم میں ہو گا اور آب جاری نجاست گرنے سے ناپاک نہیں ہوتا۔ دوسرا قیاس یہ ہے کہ پانی پاک ہی نہ ہو، کیونکہ نجاست گرنے کی وجہ سے پانی ناپاک ہو گیا، کنویں کے اطراف، دیواریں اور کیچڑ نا پاک ہو گئے ، اب جتنا پانی نکالیں گے اسی قدر نیچے سے آتا رہے گا اور وہ پانی اس ناپاک پانی سے مل کر خود نا پاک ہو جائے گا، پس یہ سلسلہ چلتا رہے گا اور کنویں کا پانی کبھی پاک نہیں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کنویں کے مسائل میں قیاس کا کوئی دخل نہیں ہے، یہ اتباع آثار پر مبنی ہیں۔ سلف کے اجماع کی وجہ محض کنویں کا پانی نکالنے سے کنواں پاک ہو جائے گا، اس کی دیوار میں دھونا اور کیچڑ کا نکالنا واجب نہیں ہے۔ چھوٹے جانور میں کتنے ڈول نکالے جائیں؟ اس سے بڑے میں کتنے ڈول نکالے جائیں گے؟ کس صورت میں پورا پانی نکالا جائے گا؟ طہارت کس طرح ہوگی؟ یہ سب آثار پر مبنی ہیں۔
وَمَسَائِلُ الْآبَارِ مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى اتِّبَاعِ الْآثَارِ دُونَ الْقِيَاسِ لأَنَّ الْقِيَاسَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ : إِمَّا أَنْ تُطَمَّ الْبِرُ كُلُّهَا طَمَّا لِتَنجُسِ الْأَوْحَالِ وَالْجُدْرَانِ، وَإِمَّا أَلَّا تَنجُسَ أَبَدًا إِذِ الْمَاءُ يَنْبُعُ مِنْ أَسْفَلِهِ فَكَانَ كَالْمَاءِ الْجَارِي قَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: اتَّفَقَ رَأْيِي وَرَأْي أَبِي يُوسُفَ أَنَّ مَاءَ الْبِثْرِ فِي حُكْمِ الْمَاءِ الْجَارِى إِلَّا أَنَّا تَرَكْنَا الْقِيَاسَ وَاتَّبَعْنَا الْآثَارَ.
العناية كتاب الطهارة، فصل في البئر، ج ۱ ص ۹۹، ط: دار الفكر وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ لَا تَطْهُرَ أَصَلا لاخْتِلاطِ النَّجَاسَةِ بِجَمِيعِ مَا فِيهَا مِنْ الْأَحْجَارِ وَالْأَخْشَابِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا وَيَتَعَذَّرُ الْعُسُلُ أَوْ لَا يَتَنَجَّسُ اعْتِبَارًا بِالْمَاءِ الْجَارِي لِأَنَّهَا كُلَّمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ أَعْلَاهَا يَنْبُعُ مِنْ أَسْفَلِهَا لَكِنْ تُرِكَ الْقِيَاسُ لِلْآثَارِ ; وَلِهَذَا قِيلَ : مَسَائِلُ الْآبَارِ مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى اتِّبَاعِ الْآثَارِ
(26) Indeed, well issues are built upon following narrations, not analogy. Translation: The issues of wells are based on following narrations, not on analogy. Explanation: The gist of the principle is that all matters of wells are founded on following narrations; analogy has no role in it. Analogy in this chapter is not reliable because there are two conflicting analogies regarding well water. One analogy requires that the water does not become impure at all, because water continuously wells up from below, so well water is like flowing water, and flowing water does not become impure by the falling of impurity. The second analogy is that the water does not become pure at all, because due to the falling of impurity, the water became impure; the walls, sides, and mud of the well became impure. Now as much water as is removed, that much comes from below, and that water mixing with the impure water becomes impure itself. So this chain continues and the well water never becomes pure. Therefore, analogy has no role in well issues; they are built on following narrations. Due to the consensus of the Salaf, the well becomes pure simply by removing the water; washing its walls and removing the mud are not obligatory. How many buckets should be removed for a small animal? How many for a large animal? In which case should all the water be removed? How is purification achieved? All these are based on narrations. And well issues are built upon following narrations, not analogy, because analogy leads to one of two things: either the entire well must be completely emptied due to the impurity of the sludge and walls, or it never becomes impure at all because water springs from its bottom, so it is like flowing water. Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: My opinion and the opinion of Abu Yusuf agreed that the water of the well is in the ruling of flowing water, except that we left analogy and followed the narrations. Al-Inayah, Book of Purity, Section on the Well (And analogy is that it does not become pure at all due to the mixing of impurity with all the stones and wood and other things in it, and it is impossible to clean thoroughly, or it does not become impure by analogy with flowing water because whenever water is taken from its top, it springs from its bottom. But analogy was abandoned for the narrations; therefore it is said: Well issues are built upon following narrations.)
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle (qawida) for the Hanafi rules regarding purification of wells after contamination. The principle states that well issues (masa'il al-abi-ar) are based on following narrations (athar) from the Prophet and the Salaf, not on qiyas (analogy). The reason given is that qiyas would lead to two contradictory outcomes: one would judge the water as never impure (because it is similar to flowing water), and the other would judge it as never becoming pure (because all parts are contaminated). Since qiyas is inconclusive, the scholars relied on transmitted reports (athar) which specify the number of buckets to be removed based on the type of animal that fell in. Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf agreed that, in principle, well water is like flowing water, but they nevertheless left qiyas and followed the narrations. This principle highlights that in certain areas of fiqh, transmitted evidence overrides analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
آبار (abi-ar): Wells; specifically referring to wells of water and their rulings after impurity falls in.
أثار (athar): Narrations; specifically the transmitted rulings from the Prophet and the Companions regarding well purification.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here it is deemed unreliable because it leads to contradictory results.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Hanafi scholars abandon qiyas (analogy) when ruling on well purification?
A) Because qiyas is always unreliable
B) Because qiyas led to two contradictory conclusions
C) Because they had no narrations on the topic
D) Because flowing water and well water are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that well purification rulings are based solely on narrations, not analogy, resolves the contradiction inherent in qiyas and ensures practical, reliable rules for taharah.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Rulings of Wells Are Based on Following Traditions, Not Analogy
Principle that well rulings follow athar, not qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school abandons qiyas in well rulings and state the operative principle based on athar.

Core Idea
Well rulings are based on transmitted traditions (athar), not analogy (qiyas), because qiyas yields two contradictory results.

This passage introduces a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh concerning the purification of wells. Rather than rely on analogical reasoning (qiyas), the Hanafi school follows traditions (athar) transmitted from the Prophet and Companions. Understanding why qiyas is abandoned here helps you appreciate how the Hanafi method prioritizes transmitted knowledge over speculative analogy when the latter is inconclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What exactly is being ruled upon? The purification of well water after impurity falls into it.
2
Recognize the two conflicting analogies
One analogy says water never becomes impure (like flowing water); the other says it never becomes pure (entire well contaminated). Both are extreme.
3
Understand why qiyas is set aside
Qiyas fails to give a single sound ruling, so it is abandoned.
4
Identify the alternative method: ittiba' al-athar
The Hanafi school relies on transmitted rulings from the Salaf.
5
Apply the principle
The detailed rulings (how many buckets, etc.) are all based on athar, not qiyas.

فصل في البئر
(٢٦) إِنَّ مَسَائِلَ الْآبَارِ مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى اتِّبَاعِ الْآثَارِ دُونَ الْقِيَاسِ . ترجمہ: کنویں کے مسائل اتباع آثار پر مبنی ہیں نہ کہ قیاس پر۔
تشریح: اصول کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ کنویں کے تمام مسائل التباع آثار پر بنی ہیں ، اس میں قیاس کا کوئی دخل نہیں ہے، قیاس اس باب میں اس لئے معتبر نہیں کہ کنویں کے پانی میں دو قیاس متضاد ہیں۔ ایک قیاس کا تقاضہ تو یہ ہے کہ پانی ناپاک ہی نہ ہو اس لئے کہ کنویں میں نیچے سے مسلسل پانی نکلتا رہتا ہے، لہذا کنویں کا پانی آپ جاری کے حکم میں ہو گا اور آب جاری نجاست گرنے سے ناپاک نہیں ہوتا۔ دوسرا قیاس یہ ہے کہ پانی پاک ہی نہ ہو، کیونکہ نجاست گرنے کی وجہ سے پانی ناپاک ہو گیا، کنویں کے اطراف، دیواریں اور کیچڑ نا پاک ہو گئے ، اب جتنا پانی نکالیں گے اسی قدر نیچے سے آتا رہے گا اور وہ پانی اس ناپاک پانی سے مل کر خود نا پاک ہو جائے گا، پس یہ سلسلہ چلتا رہے گا اور کنویں کا پانی کبھی پاک نہیں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کنویں کے مسائل میں قیاس کا کوئی دخل نہیں ہے، یہ اتباع آثار پر مبنی ہیں۔ سلف کے اجماع کی وجہ محض کنویں کا پانی نکالنے سے کنواں پاک ہو جائے گا، اس کی دیوار میں دھونا اور کیچڑ کا نکالنا واجب نہیں ہے۔ چھوٹے جانور میں کتنے ڈول نکالے جائیں؟ اس سے بڑے میں کتنے ڈول نکالے جائیں گے؟ کس صورت میں پورا پانی نکالا جائے گا؟ طہارت کس طرح ہوگی؟ یہ سب آثار پر مبنی ہیں۔
وَمَسَائِلُ الْآبَارِ مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى اتِّبَاعِ الْآثَارِ دُونَ الْقِيَاسِ لأَنَّ الْقِيَاسَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ : إِمَّا أَنْ تُطَمَّ الْبِرُ كُلُّهَا طَمَّا لِتَنجُسِ الْأَوْحَالِ وَالْجُدْرَانِ، وَإِمَّا أَلَّا تَنجُسَ أَبَدًا إِذِ الْمَاءُ يَنْبُعُ مِنْ أَسْفَلِهِ فَكَانَ كَالْمَاءِ الْجَارِي قَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: اتَّفَقَ رَأْيِي وَرَأْي أَبِي يُوسُفَ أَنَّ مَاءَ الْبِثْرِ فِي حُكْمِ الْمَاءِ الْجَارِى إِلَّا أَنَّا تَرَكْنَا الْقِيَاسَ وَاتَّبَعْنَا الْآثَارَ.
العناية كتاب الطهارة، فصل في البئر، ج ۱ ص ۹۹، ط: دار الفكر وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ لَا تَطْهُرَ أَصَلا لاخْتِلاطِ النَّجَاسَةِ بِجَمِيعِ مَا فِيهَا مِنْ الْأَحْجَارِ وَالْأَخْشَابِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا وَيَتَعَذَّرُ الْعُسُلُ أَوْ لَا يَتَنَجَّسُ اعْتِبَارًا بِالْمَاءِ الْجَارِي لِأَنَّهَا كُلَّمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ أَعْلَاهَا يَنْبُعُ مِنْ أَسْفَلِهَا لَكِنْ تُرِكَ الْقِيَاسُ لِلْآثَارِ ؛ وَلِهَذَا قِيلَ : مَسَائِلُ الْآبَارِ مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى اتِّبَاعِ الْآثَارِ
=
Section on the well. (26) Indeed the rulings of wells are built upon following traditions, not upon analogy. Translation: The rulings of wells are based on following traditions, not on analogy. Explanation: The gist of the principle is that all the rulings of wells are built upon following traditions; analogy has no role in this. Analogy is not considered in this chapter because there are two contradictory analogies regarding the water of the well. One analogy requires that the water not become impure at all, because water continuously flows from below in the well; therefore the water of the well would be in the ruling of flowing water, and flowing water does not become impure by the falling of impurity. The second analogy is that the water never becomes pure, because due to the falling of impurity the water became impure, the sides, walls, and mud of the well became impure. Now as much water as is removed, that much will come from below, and that water will mix with this impure water and itself become impure. So this chain continues and the water of the well will never become pure. Therefore analogy has no role in the rulings of wells; they are based on following traditions. Due to the consensus of the predecessors, merely by removing the water the well becomes pure; washing its walls and removing the mud is not obligatory. In the case of small animals, how many buckets should be removed? In the case of larger ones, how many buckets? In which case should all the water be removed? How is purification done? All of this is based on traditions. And the rulings of wells are built upon following traditions, not on analogy, because analogy leads to one of two things: either the entire well must be completely filled up due to the impurity of the mud and walls, or it never becomes impure at all because water flows from its bottom, so it is like flowing water. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: My opinion and the opinion of Abu Yusuf agreed that the water of the well is in the ruling of flowing water, but we abandoned analogy and followed the traditions. Al-‘Inayah, Kitab al-Tahara, Fasl fi al-Bi’r, vol. 1, p. 99, Dar al-Fikr: And analogy is that it never becomes pure at all due to the mixing of impurity with all the stones, wood, etc., and washing becomes impossible, or it does not become impure, considering it like flowing water because whenever water is taken from its top, it flows from its bottom. But analogy was abandoned for the traditions, and therefore it is said: The rulings of wells are built upon following traditions.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage states a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding the purification of wells. The author begins by declaring that the rulings concerning wells are not derived through qiyas (analogy) but rather through athar (traditions/reports from the Prophet and the Companions). The explanation in Urdu elaborates that there are two conflicting analogies: one suggests the water never becomes impure (by analogy with flowing water that purifies itself), and the other suggests it never becomes pure (since the entire well—walls, mud, and water—is contaminated, and removing water only brings up more impure water). Both analogies are problematic, so the scholars abandoned qiyas and relied on transmitted reports (athar). The text then quotes Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani confirming that he and Abu Yusuf agree that the well water is, in principle, like flowing water, but they set aside that analogy and followed the athar. Finally, a quote from al-‘Inayah reiterates the two conflicting analogies and concludes that qiyas is abandoned for athar. The key takeaway is that the detailed rulings on wells—how much water to remove for different animals, whether walls need washing, etc.—are all based on transmitted knowledge, not logical deduction.

Key Terms
اتباع الآثار (Ittiba' al-athar): Following the transmitted reports from the Prophet and Companions regarding ruling on wells.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in Islamic law; here it is abandoned for well rulings due to conflicting results.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are the rulings of wells based on following traditions (athar) rather than qiyas?
A) Because wells are not covered by any primary texts.
B) Because qiyas leads to two contradictory conclusions, so it is set aside.
C) Because water always becomes impure in wells.
D) Because there is no consensus on the matter.

Key Takeaway
Well rulings are derived from transmitted traditions, not analogical reasoning, because qiyas yields contradictory results.

Taharah — Purification

Small Amount of Dung in Wells is Pardoned by Istihsan
Ruling that small impurities in wells are pardoned by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school pardons a small amount of dung in wells using istihsan, and distinguish this from the qiyas ruling.

Core Idea
Small unavoidable impurities are pardoned to prevent hardship – istihsan overrides qiyas due to necessity.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi application of istihsan (juristic preference) in the context of water purity. The issue is whether a small amount of animal dung (e.g., one or two pellets) falling into a well makes the water impure. While the default ruling (qiyas) would declare it impure, the Hanafi school makes an exception based on necessity (darura) to avoid constant hardship. We will trace the reasoning behind this ruling, the proofs cited, and how it applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is the falling of a small amount of dung (e.g., one or two pellets from camels or goats) into a well. The ruling is that the water remains pure (tahir) and can be used without purification. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is 'afuw (pardoned) – the impurity is overlooked.
2
Locate the Two Proofs: Qiyas and Istihsan
The text presents two competing proofs. Qiyas says water becomes impure because it is a small quantity (qalil) and any impurity in it makes it impure. Istihsan says it does not become impure because of necessity. Identify each proof and its basis.
The qiyas analogy compares the well to a vessel (inā').
3
Understand the Necessity Argument (Istihsan)
The necessity arises because desert wells often lack covers, and animals grazing nearby drop dung which the wind blows into the well. If every small amount were impure, it would cause hardship (haraj), contradicting the Quranic principle of removing hardship (22:78).
The text cites the verse 'وَمَا جَعَلَ عَلَيْكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ مِنْ حَرَج' (And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship).
4
Note the Scope of the Ruling
The istihsan applies to all types of dung (camel, goat, horse, etc.) and regardless of whether the dung is wet or dry, whole or broken. The key factor is the small amount and the presence of necessity.
If the amount is large (kathir), necessity does not apply and the water becomes impure.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The difference between the Hanafi position and the strict qiyas position is clear: Hanafi allows using the water with one or two dung pellets, while the opposing position (often associated with Shafi'i or literalist approach) would require removing the impurity or draining the well. This has significant practical implications for communities relying on open wells.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, chosen by the author of al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارة، ج ا ص ۱۱۸، ط: دار الكتاب الاسلامي]
فجعل القليل عفوا للضرورة
(۲۷) إِنَّ الْقَلِيلَ جُعِلَ عَفُوًا لِلضَّرُورَةِ.
ترجمہ: بے شک قلیل مقدار کو ضرورت کی وجہ سے معاف کیا گیا ہے۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل کے ذکر کیا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اگر کنویں میں اونٹ یا بکری کی ایک یا دو مینگنیاں گر پڑیں تو قیا سا پانی ناپاک ہو جائے گا اور استحساناً پانی ناپاک نہیں ہوگا، ایک دومینگنیوں سے مراد قلیل مقدار ہے۔ قیاس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ ماء قلیل ہے، اور ماء قلیل میں نجاست گرنے سے وہ ناپاک ہو جاتا ہے، خواہ نجاست قلیل ہو یا کثیر ہو۔ استحسان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عام طور پر جنگلوں اور صحراؤں میں جو کنویں ہوتے ہیں ان کے سروں اور کناروں پر کوئی چیز روکنے والی نہیں ہوتی، مویشی جب پانی پینے آتے ہیں تو عموماً ان کے اردگرد مینگنیاں کر دیتے ہیں، پھر ہوا کی وجہ سے وہ مینگنیاں کنویں میں گر جاتی ہیں ، اس لئے ضرورت کی وجہ سے قلیل مقدار معاف ہے، چونکہ کثیر میں ضرورت نہیں ہے اس لئے وہ معاف نہیں ہے۔
= حَتَّى إِذَا خَرَجَ الْوَاجِبُ مِنْهَا حُكِمَ بِطَهَارَةِ جَمِيعِ مَا فِيهَا وَدَلْوِهَا وَيَدِ النَّازِحِ
مجمع الأنهر كتاب الطهارة ، ج ۱ ص ۳۳، ط: دار إحياء التراث]
الهداية كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى البئر، ج ۱ ص ۴۰، ط: رحمانيه
(قَوْلُهُ: لَا بِبَعْرَتَى إِبِلٍ وَغَنَمٍ أَى لَا يُنْزَحُ مَاء الْبِشِّرِ بِوُقُوعِ بَعْرَتَى إِبِلٍ وَغَنَمٍ فِيهَا، وَهَذَا اسْتِحْسَانُ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يَتَنَجَّسَ الْمَاء مُطْلَقًا لِوُقُوعِ النَّجَاسَةِ فِي الْمَاءِ الْقَلِيلِ كَالْإِنَاءِ وَذُكِرَ لِلاسْتِحْسَانِ طَرِيقَتَانِ الْأُولَى وَاخْتَارَهَا صَاحِبُ الْهِدَايَةِ مُقْتَصِرًا عَلَيْهَا أَنَّ آبَارَ الْفَلَوَاتِ لَيْسَ لَهَا رُءُوسٌ حَاجِزَةٌ وَالْمَوَاشِي تَبْعَرُ حَوْلَهَا وَيُلْقِيهَا الرِّيحُ فِيهَا فَجُعِلَ الْقَلِيلُ عَفْوًا لِلضَّرُورَةِ وَلَا ضَرُورَةَ فِي الْكَثِيرِ وَلَا فَرْقَ عَلَى هَذَا بَيْنَ الرَّطْبِ وَالْيَابِسِ، وَالصَّحِيحِ وَالْمُنْكَسِرِ، وَالرَّوْثِ وَالْبَعْرِ وَالْخِشَى، لِأَنَّ الضَّرُورَةَ تَشْمَلُ الْكُلَّ.
البحر الرائق: كتاب الطهارة، ج ا ص ۱۱۸، ط: دار الكتاب الاسلامي] فجعل القليل عفوا للضرورة أى فإذا كان كذلك جعل القليل من البعر عفوا لأجل الضرورة فلو أفسده القليل أدى إلى الحرج (وَمَا جَعَلَ عَلَيْكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ مِنْ حَرَج) وهو الذي ذكره هو أحد وجهى الاستحسان. قال في المبسوط للاستحسان وجهان أحدهما: أن في القليل ضرورة، ووجها ما ذكره المصنف والوجه الثاني لم يذكره المصنف وهو أن البعر شيء صلب
Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq: Kitab al-Tahara, vol. 1, p. 118, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami: So the small amount was made pardoned due to necessity. (27) Indeed the small amount was made pardoned due to necessity. Translation: Without doubt, a small amount is excused due to necessity. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah mentioned the above principle as a proof, the gist of which is that if one or two dung pellets of a camel or goat fall into a well, then by qiyas the water becomes impure, but by istihsan the water does not become impure. By one or two pellets, a small amount is meant. The proof of qiyas is that this is a small amount of water, and when impurity falls into a small amount of water, it becomes impure, whether the impurity is small or large. The proof of istihsan is that in forests and deserts, wells usually have no covers, and when animals come to drink, they commonly drop dung around them; then due to the wind, those dung pellets fall into the well. Therefore, a small amount is excused due to necessity. Since there is no necessity in a large amount, it is not excused. = Until when the obligatory (amount) is taken out, it is ruled that all that is in it is pure, including its bucket and the hand of the drawer. Majma‘ al-Anhur, Kitab al-Tahara, vol. 1, p. 33, Dar Ihya’ al-Turath. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Tahara, Fasl fi al-Bi’r, vol. 1, p. 40, Rahmaniyya. (His saying: Not by two dung pellets of camels and goats), i.e., the water of the well is not drained due to the fall of two dung pellets of camels and goats into it. This is istihsan, and the qiyas is that the water becomes impure absolutely due to the fall of impurity into a small amount of water, like a vessel. Two methods are mentioned for istihsan: the first, which the author of al-Hidayah chose and confined himself to, is that the wells of the deserts have no covers, and livestock dung around them, and the wind throws it in; so the small amount was made pardoned due to necessity, and there is no necessity in the large amount. There is no difference in this between wet and dry, whole and broken, dung and pellet and dung of horses, because necessity includes all. Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, Kitab al-Tahara, vol. 1, p. 118, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami: So the small amount was made pardoned due to necessity, i.e., if that is the case, then a small amount of dung was pardoned due to necessity; if the small amount caused corruption, it would lead to difficulty. (And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship [22:78]) This is what he mentioned, and it is one of the two aspects of istihsan. He said in al-Mabsut that there are two aspects of istihsan: one is that there is necessity in the small amount, and the second aspect is what the author mentioned, and the second aspect he did not mention, which is that dung is solid.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage addresses the istihsan ruling that a small amount of dung (e.g., one or two pellets) falling into a well does not render the water impure, despite the qiyas that any impurity in a small quantity of water makes it impure. The author explains that the Hanafi school prefers istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas in this case due to necessity (darura). The necessity arises because wells in open deserts often have no covers, and animal dung frequently falls in, so if every small amount were considered impure, it would cause hardship (haraj). The text quotes the Quranic verse "And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship" (22:78) as a basis for this istihsan. The passage then elaborates that this ruling applies regardless of whether the dung is wet or dry, whole or broken, and includes all types of dung (camel, goat, horse, etc.). The exception is when the amount is large; then the qiyas applies and the water becomes impure. The quote from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq mentions two aspects of istihsan: the necessity reason and another reason (not detailed) that dung is solid. The teacher should emphasize that this is a clear example of the Hanafi use of istihsan to alleviate hardship, grounded in the general principle of removing difficulty.

Key Terms
عَفُوًا (afwan): Pardoned; a small amount of impurity that is overlooked and does not affect the purity of water.
ضرورة (darura): Necessity; the excuse that makes a small amount of impurity pardonable to avoid hardship.
بعر (ba‘r): Dung pellets from camels, goats, etc.; a common impurity in desert wells.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling, often based on necessity or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, what is the primary reason for pardoning a small amount of dung in a well?
A) Because dung is dry and does not mix with water.
B) Because of necessity (darura) to avoid constant hardship.
C) Because the water is considered large in quantity.
D) Because the well owner can easily remove the dung.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to pardon small unavoidable impurities in wells to prevent hardship, overriding the general qiyas that would declare the water impure.

The Urine of Halal-Meat Animals: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on urine purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the two Sheikhs and Imam Muhammad on the urine of halal-meat animals, explain the evidence used by each side, and identify the mufta bihi position with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
The urine of halal-meat animals: Imam Muhammad deems it pure; the two Sheikhs deem it impure — the mufta bihi follows the majority.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a well-known difference of opinion between Imam Muhammad and the two Sheikhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding the urine of animals whose meat is permissible to eat. This dispute has a direct practical impact on the purity of water, as illustrated by the case of a goat urinating into a well. In this lesson, we will examine the arguments on both sides, the evidence cited, and the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the two opposing positions within the Hanafi school: the two Sheikhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rule the urine as impure (najis); Imam Muhammad rules it as pure (tahir).
2
Examine the evidence
Imam Muhammad cites the hadith of the 'Urayniyyun, where the Prophet (ﷺ) permitted them to drink camel urine. The two Sheikhs interpret this as a specific dispensation, not a general rule.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its meaning is also open to interpretation.
3
Analyse the interpretation
Understand why the two Sheikhs reject the general application: they argue the permission was for medicinal treatment, so it does not establish that urine is inherently pure.
This is a classic example of takhsis (specification) of a general indication.
4
Apply to a practical case
Consider a goat urinating in a well. According to the two Sheikhs, all water must be removed because any impurity contaminates the entire body of water. According to Imam Muhammad, the water remains pure unless the urine dominates.
This thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) shows how the difference matters in daily life.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of the two Sheikhs: the urine of halal-meat animals is impure. This is the view adopted in the later fatwa works.
Authority weight: the majority and the practice of the school.

Principles of Hidayah Volume 1 (28) Indeed the urine of what is eaten for its meat is pure according to Muhammad and impure according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Translation: The urine of an animal whose meat is eaten is pure according to Imam Muhammad, and according to the two Sheikhs it is impure. Explanation: The author of Hidayah mentioned a disputed issue before this principle, and the original reason for this disputed issue is explained by this principle. The issue is: if a goat urinates in a well, according to the two Sheikhs the entire water of the well will be removed, and according to Imam Muhammad, removal of the water is not necessary; however, if the urine dominates the water, then in that case the water will no longer be purifying but it will be pure (tahir). The original reason for this difference is explained by this principle: the urine of an animal whose meat is eaten is pure according to Imam Muhammad, so if it falls into a small amount of water, it will not make it impure, and it is permissible to perform wudu with it, etc. According to the two Sheikhs, the urine of a halal-meat animal is impure, so if even a drop falls into the water, the water becomes impure. Therefore, if a goat urinated in the well, all the water will be removed. = And on its surface there is moisture in the intestines like a membrane, and in it there is viscosity that prevents water from entering its folds. [Al-Binaayah, Book of Taharah, Chapter on the Well, vol 1 p 436] Because if the alloy is small, it is not considered, because silver is not impressed except with a small alloy. So the small amount is excused, not the large, and the separator between them is dominance. Whichever is dominant is considered. [Al-Binaayah, Book of Zakat, Chapter on Silver, vol 3 p 373] [Al-Hidayah, Book of Taharah, Chapter on the Well, vol 1 p 41] So there is no disagreement that the urine of every animal whose meat is not eaten is impure. And there is disagreement regarding the urine of what is eaten for its meat. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf said: it is impure. Muhammad said: it is pure, so that if it falls into a small body of water, it does not spoil it, and wudu can be performed from it as long as it does not dominate. And he argued with what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he permitted the 'Urayniyyun to drink the urine of the sadaqah camels and their milk, along with his statement (peace be upon him): Verily Allah did not make...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It presents a well-known khilaf within the Hanafi school regarding the urine of animals whose meat is halal (ma'kul al-lahm). The practical consequence appears in the case of a goat urinating into a well: according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the two Sheikhs), the urine is najis (impure), so all the well water must be removed. According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, the urine is tahir (pure), so the water remains pure and can be used for wudu unless the urine dominates. Imam Muhammad's evidence is the hadith of the 'Urayniyyun, where the Prophet (ﷺ) permitted them to drink camel urine for medicinal purposes. The two Sheikhs interpret this as a specific permission due to necessity, not a general ruling. The passage also includes an analogy from silver alloys: small impurities are overlooked. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of the two Sheikhs: the urine of halal-meat animals is impure.

Key Terms
مَا يُؤْكَلُ لَحْمُهُ (ma yukalu lahmuhu): Animals whose meat is permissible to eat in Islam, such as sheep, goats, cows, chickens, etc.
شَيْخَانِ (shaykhayn): The two Sheikhs: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, distinguished from Imam Muhammad.
بَوْل (bawal): Urine, a liquid waste product of animals.
عُرَنِيِّينَ (Urayniyyun): A group of people from the 'Uraynah tribe who came to Medina and were permitted by the Prophet to drink camel urine for medical treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi is that the urine of halal-meat animals is impure, based on the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, despite Imam Muhammad's differing opinion.

The Principle of Equivalence: Ruling for Animals in a Well
Legal principle and its application to well purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of equivalence (mu'adala) to determine the water-purification ruling for any animal that falls into a well before decomposition.

Core Idea
When body size equals, ruling equals: the mouse's 20-bucket obligation applies to any animal of similar body.

In this passage, the Hanafi jurists establish a powerful analogy: the ruling for a mouse that falls into a well extends to every animal equal in body size. You will learn not only the specific ruling but also the legal principle that makes such extensions valid. This is a core tool for understanding how rulings expand beyond their textual basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base case and its ruling
First, identify the base case: a mouse falls into a well and is removed before decomposition. The ruling for purifying the well is that 20 buckets must be drawn (wajib) and up to 30 is recommended.
The base ruling comes from transmitted reports (athar).
2
Understand the principle of equivalence
The text states 'when something equals something else (in body), it takes its ruling.' The key attribute is body size (juththa). If another animal has the same body size as the mouse, it is considered equal (mu'adala).
Equivalence is not in every attribute, only in the one that affects the ruling, here body size.
3
Apply equivalence to new cases
Apply the principle to animals like sparrow, weasel (godiya), or beetle (bheinga). Since these equal the mouse in body size, they take the same ruling: 20 buckets wajib, 30 mustahabb.
The ruling is not limited to the mouse; it extends via qiyas.
4
Note the conditions
The equivalence applies only when the animal has not swollen or burst. If decomposition has started, the ruling changes (not covered in this lesson).
5
Recognize the broader legal tool
This principle is a manifestation of qiyas (analogy) in Hanafi fiqh. It shows how a ruling can be transferred from an original case to a new case when the effective cause (illa) is shared—here the illa is body size causing similar water contamination.
Other schools use similar but not identical methods; the Hanafi emphasis on juththa is distinctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: جب کوئی شی دوسری شی کے (جسم میں برابر ہو ) تو حکم بھی ایک ہوگا۔ تشریح: چوہا اگر کنویں میں گر جائے تو اس کے نکالنے کے بعد میں ڈول پانی نکالنے سے کنواں پاک ہو جائے گا، جب کہ وہ پھولا پھٹا نہ ہو۔ اسی طرح وہ جانور جو جسم و جھٹے میں چوہے کے ہم مثل ہیں جیسے گودیا، بھینگا وغیرہ اگر یہ بھی کنویں میں گر جائیں تو ان کا حکم بھی وہی ہے جو چوہے کا ہے یعنی میں ڈول پانی نکالنا واجب ہے اور میں ڈول تک نکالنا مستحب ہے۔ چوہے کا حکم روایت میں موجود ہے، لہذا دوسرے جانوروں کو جتنے میں ہم مثل ہونے کی وجہ سے چو ہے والا حکم دیا گیا ہے۔

(30) Indeed, when something equals something else (in body), it takes its ruling. Translation: When one thing equals another in body, its ruling will also be the same. Explanation: If a mouse falls into a well, after removing it, drawing 20 buckets of water will purify the well, provided it has not swollen or burst. Similarly, animals that resemble the mouse in body and size, such as the godiya and bheinga, if they fall into the well, their ruling is the same as the mouse's, i.e., drawing 20 buckets is obligatory, and drawing up to 30 buckets is recommended. The ruling for the mouse is found in the narration, so other animals are given the same ruling due to their resemblance in body. = If someone had a nosebleed and wrote al-Fatihah with blood on his forehead and nose, it is permissible for seeking cure, and with urine also if he knows it contains a cure, there is no harm, but it has not been transmitted; and this is because prohibition is lifted in seeking cure, like the permissibility of wine and carrion for the thirsty and hungry. End from al-Bahr. And Sayyidi Abdul Ghani stated that there is no apparent disagreement in their statements due to their agreement on permissibility for necessity, and the condition of knowledge by the author of al-Nihayah does not contradict the condition of cure by later scholars, and therefore my father said in Sharh al-Durar: His statement 'no' for treatment is interpreted as uncertain, but otherwise its permissibility with certainty is agreed upon, as stated in al-Musaffa. I say: It is apparent and consistent with what passed in the inference from the Imam's statement: but you know that the doctors' statement does not produce knowledge. And it is apparent that experimentation produces preponderant conjecture without certainty, unless they mean by knowledge preponderant conjecture, and it is common in their speech. Reflect. Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Tahara, Bab al-Miyah, Matlab fi al-Tadawi bil-Muharram, vol. 1, p. 210; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Taharat, Fasl fi al-Bi'r, vol. 1, pp. 41, 42. And the sparrow and the like equal the mouse in body, so they take its ruling, meaning the ruling of the mouse. And he indicated by this that the effect mentioned, even if it is reported regarding the mouse, includes every animal the size of the mouse, so it takes its ruling, so 20 to 30 buckets are obligatory. [Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl fi al-Bi'r, vol. 1, p. 449, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah: It is reported regarding the mouse: 20 buckets, and the sparrow and the like equal the mouse in body, so they take its ruling. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Taharah, Ma' al-Bi'r, vol. 1, p. 28, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra]
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines and applies the legal principle of equivalence (al-mu'adala) in Hanafi fiqh. The principle states that when two things are alike in a relevant attribute (here, body size), they share the same ruling. The commentator begins by stating the principle in Arabic: 'When something equals something else in body, it takes its ruling.' Then he provides a practical example: if a mouse falls into a well and is removed before decomposition, the well is purified by drawing 20 buckets of water. The ruling is based on transmitted reports. Then he extends the ruling to animals that resemble the mouse in body, such as a sparrow, godiya, or bheinga. The author cites several sources: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Radd al-Muhtar, al-Hidayah, al-Binayah, and Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, all confirming the principle. A secondary discussion about treating with impure substances (blood, urine) is included, but the primary focus is the equivalence principle. The key point: the ruling is not limited to the mouse; any animal of similar size (e.g., sparrow, weasel) takes the same ruling of 20-30 buckets.

Key Terms
عدالة (mu'adala): Equivalence in the attribute that affects the ruling, here body size (juththa).
جثة (juththa): The physical body or carcass; the basis for equivalence in this ruling.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of mu'adala allows the ruling for a mouse in a well to be extended to any animal of equal body size, ensuring consistent purification rules.

Certainty Is Not Removed by Doubt: The Well Water Dispute
Explains the maxim and its application in the well water dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding retrospective impurity of well water, state the mufta bihi position, and apply the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt'.

Core Idea
Certainty of purity remains until equal certainty proves impurity – doubt cannot overturn it.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute from al-Hidayah on well water. When a dead animal is found in a well but the time of its fall is unknown, does the water become retroactively impure? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes, based on the visible cause of death in water. The Sahibayn disagree, holding that the prior certainty of purity is not removed by doubt. The author of al-Hidayah sides with the Sahibayn, using the famous maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt'. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply legal maxims and appreciate how usul principles operate in fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
Consider the scenario: a dead animal is found in a well, but the time it fell is unknown. The core legal question is whether the water is considered impure from the past, requiring repetition of prayers performed with that water.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Imam Abu Hanifa rules that prayers performed with the water must be repeated (one day/night if not decomposed, three days if decomposed). His proof: death in water has a visible cause (falling in), and the carcass is attributed to that cause. Analogies: a wound causing delayed death is attributed to the wound.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that no repetition is required until the time of the fall is known with certainty. Their proof: the water was certainly pure before, and doubt about when it became impure does not remove that certainty. They analogize to a person finding impurity on his garment without knowing when it occurred – no past prayers are repeated.
4
Trace the maxim's application
The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' is directly invoked by the Sahibayn. The prior certain purity of the water is not overridden by the doubt of whether the animal fell earlier. Only if the exact time is known does certainty arise, and then the ruling changes.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, one repeats prayers of one day/night or three days; under Sahibayn, no repetition until the time is known. Al-Hidayah prefers the Sahibayn's view, making it the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۱) إِنَّ الْيَقِينَ لَا يَزُولُ بالشَّكِ.
ترجمہ: یقین شک کی وجہ سے زائل نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح: صاحب ہدایہ نے اصول مذکورہ کو حضرات صاحبین کے مسلک کے ثبوت پر بطورِ دلیل کے پیش کیا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اگر لوگوں نے کسی کنویں میں مرا ہوا جانور دیکھا لیکن یہ معلوم نہ ہو سکا کہ کب گرا ہے اور ابھی تک پھولا پھٹا بھی نہیں ہے، تو اس صورت میں حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر اس کنویں سے وضو کر کے نمازیں پڑھی ہیں تو ایک دن اور ایک رات کی نمازیں لوٹانا ضروری ہے اور جس چیز کو اس کنویں کا پانی لگا ہوا سے بھی دھویا جائے گا۔
اور اگر وہ جانور پھول گیا یا پھٹ گیا تو اس صورت میں تین دن کی نمازوں کا اعادہ کرنا ضروری ہے، یہ امام ابو حنیفہ رحمہ اللہ کا مذہب ہے، اور حضراتِ صاحبین کے نزدیک ان لوگوں پر کسی چیز کا اعادہ واجب نہیں ہے، جب تک یہ ثابت نہ ہو جائے کہ یہ جانور کب گرا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے حضرات صاحبین کے مسلک کو ثابت کرنے کے لئے اصول مذکورہ کو بطور دلیل کے ذکر کیا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ کنویں کا پانی بالیقین پاک تھا مگر اس میں مرا ہوا جانور پانے کی وجہ سے گزشتہ ایام میں اس کے ناپاک ہونے میں شک واقع ہو گیا اور اصول ہے کہ یقین شک سے زائل نہیں ہوتا ، لہذا یہاں پر یقین شک کی وجہ سے زائل نہیں ہوگا، جب تک تحقیق سے معلوم نہ ہو جائے کہ یہ جانور کب گرا ہے تو گزشتہ ایام میں اس کے ناپاک ہونے کا حکم نہیں دیا جائے گا، اور ان پر کسی چیز کا اعادہ واجب نہیں ہوگا۔
الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى البئر، ج ۱ ص ۴۲، ط: رحمانيه لأَنَّ الْيَقِينَ لَا يَزُولُ بِالشَّكِّ بَيَانُهُ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ كَانَ طَاهِرًا بِيَقِينِ وَوَقَعَ الشَّكُ فِي نَجَاسَتِهِ فِيمَا مَضَى وَالْيَقِينُ لَا يَزُولُ بِالشَّكَ فَلَا يُحْكُمُ بِالنَّجَاسَةِ إِلَّا زَمَانَ التَّيَقُنِ بِوُقُوعِ النَّجِسِ؛ لأَنَّ الْيَقِينَ يَزُولُ بِيَقِينِ مِثْلِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ الْقِيَاسُ، كَمَنْ رَأَى فِي ثَوْبِهِ نَجَاسَةً لَا يَدْرِي مَتَى أَصَابَتْهُ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُهُ إِعَادَةُ شَيْءٍ مِنْ الصَّلَوَاتِ. وَلَابِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ لِمَوْتِ الْحَيَوَانِ فِي الْبِئْرِ سَبَبًا ظَاهِرًا وَهُوَ الْوُقُوعُ فِي الْمَاءِ وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَهُ سَبَبٌ ظَاهِرٌ يُحَالُ عَلَيْهِ كَمَنْ جَرَحَ إِنْسَانًا فَلَمْ يَزَلْ صَاحِبَ فِرَاشٍ حَتَّى مَاتَ يُحَالُ بِمَوْتِهِ عَلَى الْحِرَاحَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ السَّبَبُ الظَّاهِرُ،
=
(31) Indeed, certainty is not removed by doubt. Translation: Certainty is not removed by doubt. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah presented this principle as evidence for the position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The summary is: If people see a dead animal in a well but do not know when it fell and it has not yet swollen or burst, then the ruling is that if they performed wudu and prayed with that water, they must repeat the prayers of one day and one night, and anything that touched the water must be washed. If the animal has swollen or burst, then they must repeat three days of prayers—this is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions, no repetition is obligatory on them until it is established when the animal fell. The author of al-Hidayah mentioned this principle as evidence for the position of the two companions. The summary is: The well water was certainly pure, but due to finding the dead animal, doubt arose about its impurity in the past. The principle is that certainty is not removed by doubt, so here certainty is not removed by doubt. Until it is known by investigation when the animal fell, the water will not be judged impure for the past, and no repetition is obligatory. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Taharat, Fasl fi al-Bi'r, vol. 1, p. 42: Because certainty is not removed by doubt. Its explanation: The water was pure with certainty, and doubt occurred about its impurity in the past. Certainty is not removed by doubt, so it is not judged impure except for the time when the impurity is known with certainty, because certainty is only removed by its like. And this is the analogy, like a person who sees impurity on his garment but does not know when it touched him; then no repetition of any prayer is obligatory on him. And for Abu Hanifa: The death of an animal in a well has a visible cause, which is falling into the water, and it is apparent. Everything that has a visible cause is attributed to it, like a person who wounds another, who then remains bedridden until death; his death is attributed to the wound because it is the visible cause.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the retrospective impurity of well water when a dead animal is found but the time of its fall is unknown. The author begins by stating the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (al-yaqinu la yazulu bi al-shakk). The context is from al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Taharah, Fasl fi al-Bi'r. The dispute: When a dead animal is found in a well and the time of its fall is unknown, Imam Abu Hanifa rules that if the animal is not decomposed, one day and night of prayers must be repeated; if decomposed, three days. This is because death in water has an apparent cause (falling into water), and the presence of the carcass indicates the water was impure from the time it entered. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), however, argue that the water was certainly pure before the animal was seen, and doubt about when it became impure does not remove that certainty. Therefore, no past prayers are invalidated. The Sahibayn support their view with the analogy of a person finding impurity on his garment without knowing when it occurred; he does not repeat past prayers. Al-Hidayah sides with the Sahibayn, using the maxim as evidence. This is a key application of the usul principle that certainty is not overridden by doubt. The passage also notes that this is the qiyas (analogy), while Abu Hanifa relies on a different reasoning based on apparent cause.

Key Terms
يقين (yaqin): Certainty; a state of knowledge free from doubt. In this context, the certain purity of the well water before finding the animal.
شك (shakk): Doubt; uncertainty about the state of the water in the past. It cannot remove certainty.
سبب ظاهر (sabab zahir): Visible/Apparent cause; in Abu Hanifa's argument, the death in water has an apparent cause (falling in), so it is attributed to that cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hidayah, which position is preferred in the well water dispute?
A) Abu Hanifa's position – repeat prayers of one or three days.
B) Sahibayn's position – no repetition until the time is known with certainty.
C) Both positions are equally valid and a mufti may choose.
D) Neither – the water is always considered impure from the moment the animal is found.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' protects the validity of past acts of worship when the time of impurity is uncertain, and the Sahibayn's view is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

The Well Water Dispute: Certainty and Apparent Cause in Purity Rulings
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well water impurity and reprayer obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on well water impurity, apply the principle 'certainty is not removed by doubt', and identify the practical consequences of each view regarding reprayer obligations.

Core Idea
Certainty of purity is not overcome by doubt, but an apparent cause can shift attribution.

When a dead animal is found in a well, the water's purity before discovery is certain, but the time of impurity is doubtful. The Hanafi school splits: the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) uphold the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt,' while Abu Hanifa gives weight to the apparent cause of death (falling into water) and estimates the time based on bloating. This lesson unpacks the legal principles behind each view and their practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify both views within the Hanafi school: (1) Sahibayn say no reprayer unless time of fall is certain. (2) Abu Hanifa says reprayer is required based on estimated time using apparent cause and bloating evidence.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, not a dispute between schools.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Their proof rests on the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك). Since the water was originally pure with certainty, doubt about when it became impure cannot override that certainty. They analogize to a garment with unknown impurity: no reprayer required.
This is strict qiyas (al-qiyas) according to the text.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa invokes the principle that an apparent cause (السبب الظاهر) is attributed to its effect. The animal's death in the well has an apparent cause—its falling into water. He analogizes to wounding and snakebite cases, where death is attributed to the apparent cause even if other possibilities exist.
He also uses bloating to estimate time: bloating = 3 days, no bloating = 1 day, based on janazah burial rulings.
4
Follow the time estimation method
If the animal is bloated, it indicates death at least three days earlier, so three days of prayers are repeated. If not bloated, it indicates recent death within one day and night, so only one day of prayers is repeated.
These estimates are derived from precedents in the chapter of janazah (prayer over grave).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Sahibayn, no reprayer; under Abu Hanifa, reprayer of either 1 or 3 days. This difference arises from weighing certainty against apparent cause.
The Hidayah presents both views but implicitly favors the Sahibayn by leading with their proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأَنَّ الْيَقِينَ لَا يَزُولُ بِالشَّكِّ بَيَانُهُ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ كَانَ طَاهِرًا بِيَقِينِ وَوَقَعَ الشَّكُ فِي نَجَاسَتِهِ فِيمَا مَضَى وَالْيَقِينُ لَا يَزُولُ بِالشَّكَ فَلَا يُحْكُمُ بِالنَّجَاسَةِ إِلَّا زَمَانَ التَّيَقُنِ بِوُقُوعِ النَّجِسِ؛ لأَنَّ الْيَقِينَ يَزُولُ بِيَقِينِ مِثْلِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ الْقِيَاسُ، كَمَنْ رَأَى فِي ثَوْبِهِ نَجَاسَةً لَا يَدْرِي مَتَى أَصَابَتْهُ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُهُ إِعَادَةُ شَيْءٍ مِنْ الصَّلَوَاتِ. وَلَابِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ لِمَوْتِ الْحَيَوَانِ فِي الْبِئْرِ سَبَبًا ظَاهِرًا وَهُوَ الْوُقُوعُ فِي الْمَاءِ وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَهُ سَبَبٌ ظَاهِرٌ يُحَالُ عَلَيْهِ كَمَنْ جَرَحَ إِنْسَانًا فَلَمْ يَزَلْ صَاحِبَ فِرَاشٍ حَتَّى مَاتَ يُحَالُ بِمَوْتِهِ عَلَى الْحِرَاحَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ السَّبَبُ الظَّاهِرُ، وَكَمَيِّتِ الْتَوَتْ فِي عُنُقِهِ حَيَّةٌ فَإِنَّهُ يُحَالُ بِمَوْتِهِ عَلَى نَهْشِهَا، وَإِنْ اُحْتُمِلَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَوْتُ بِغَيْرِ الْجَرْحِ وَالنَّهْشِ؛ لأنَّ الْمَوْهُومَ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الْمُحَقَّقِ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرٍ، إِلَّا أَنَّ الْانْتِفَاخَ دَلِيلُ تَقَادُمٍ الْعَهْدِ، وَأَدْنَى حَدٌ التَّقَادُمِ فِي ذَلِكَ ثَلَاثَةُ أَيَّامٍ، أَلا يُرَى أَنَّ مَنْ دُفِنَ بِلَا صَلَاةٍ يُصَلَّى عَلَى قَبْرِهِ إلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ وَلَا يُصَلَّى عَلَيْهِ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ يَنْفَسِحُ فِي هَذِهِ الْمُدَّةِ فَيُقَدَّرُ بِالثَّلَاثِ، وَعَدَمُ الانْتِفَاحَ وَالتَّفَسُّخِ دَلِيلُ قُرُبِ الْعَهْدِ فَقَدَّرُنَاهُ بِيَوْمٍ؛ لِأَنَّ أَقَلَّ الْمَقَادِيرِ فِي بَابِ الصَّلَاةِ يَوْمٌ وَلَيْلَةٌ، فَإِنَّ مَا دُونَهُ سَاعَاتٌ لَا يُمْكِنُ ضَبْطُهَا.
Because certainty is not removed by doubt. Its explanation: The water was certainly pure, and doubt occurred about its impurity in the past. Certainty is not removed by doubt, so impurity is not judged except for the time in which the occurrence of the impurity is certain. Because certainty is removed only by similar certainty, and this is the analogy (qiyas). Like a person who sees impurity on his garment but does not know when it befell him: he is not obliged to repeat any of the prayers. And Abu Hanifa holds that the death of an animal in the well has an apparent cause, which is its falling into the water, and it is apparent. And everything that has an apparent cause is attributed to it. Like a person who wounds someone, and the wounded person remains bedridden until he dies: his death is attributed to the wounding, because it is the apparent cause. And like a corpse with a snake coiled around its neck: his death is attributed to its bite, even if it is possible that death occurred from something other than the wound or the bite, because the presumed (al-mawhum) in face of the confirmed (al-muhaqqaq) is not considered. However, bloating is evidence of oldness of the time (taqadum al-'ahd). The minimum period for oldness in that case is three days. Do you not see that whoever is buried without prayer, the janazah prayer is performed over his grave up to three days, and it is not performed after that because decomposition occurs within this period, so it is estimated by three. And the absence of swelling and decomposition is evidence of recentness of the time, so we estimated it as one day, because the smallest measure in the chapter of prayer is one day and one night; anything less than that is hours which cannot be precisely determined.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of well water when a dead animal is found in it. The text begins by citing the legal maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' to support the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). According to them, since the water was originally pure with certainty, and doubt arises about when it became impure, the doubt does not override the certainty. Therefore, no prayers need be repeated unless the exact time of the animal's fall is known. The author then presents Abu Hanifa's position, which relies on a different principle: 'When something has an apparent cause, it is attributed to it.' Abu Hanifa argues that the animal's death in the well has an apparent cause—its falling into the water. Thus, the water is assumed to have become impure from that moment. However, if the animal is bloated, that indicates the death occurred more than three days earlier, so three days' worth of prayers must be repeated. If not bloated, it indicates recent death (within one day and night), so only one day's prayers are repeated. These time estimates are derived from analogous rulings in the chapter of janazah prayer (prayer over grave up to three days). The dispute is resolved by considering both principles: the Sahibayn's view is based on strict qiyas (analogy) with the case of unknown impurity on a garment, while Abu Hanifa's view gives weight to the apparent cause and is supported by istihsan (juristic preference).

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqeen): Certainty; a conviction that admits no doubt, based on definitive evidence.
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt; uncertainty about a matter, with equal probability of two possibilities.
السبب الظاهر (al-sabab al-zahir): An apparent cause that is evident and manifest, to which an effect is attributed in absence of contrary evidence.
الموهوم (al-mawhum): A presumed or imagined possibility that is weak and not legally considered when opposed to a confirmed reality (al-muhaqqaq).
الانتفاخ (al-intifakh): Bloating or swelling of a dead animal, used as evidence of time elapsed since death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of the Sahibayn when a dead animal is found in a well but its time of fall is unknown, and the animal is not bloated?
A) One day's prayers must be repeated.
B) Three days' prayers must be repeated.
C) No prayers need to be repeated until the time is known with certainty.
D) The water is considered impure from the time of discovery only.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, the Sahibayn's view (no reprayer without certainty) is often preferred in fatwa, but Abu Hanifa's view (reprayer based on apparent cause and bloating) remains a recognized alternative within the school.

The Principle of Apparent Cause in Legal Attribution
Explaining the rule of attributing effects to apparent causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of apparent cause and apply it to scenarios such as an animal in a well, a wound victim, a corpse in a village, and a snake bite to derive the legal ruling.

Core Idea
A ruling is based on the apparent cause, not on remote imaginary possibilities.

When an effect is observed but its precise cause is hidden, the Hanafi school relies on the most apparent observable cause. This lesson introduces the principle through four key examples from the chapter of purification (taharah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the opening statement: 'If a thing has an apparent cause, the ruling is attributed to it.' This is the core rule.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The apparent cause is clear and external; the actual cause may be hidden, so the law bases the ruling on what is evident.
This is a rational principle (dalil aqli) used throughout fiqh.
3
Apply Example 1: Animal in Well
A mouse falls into water and dies. The apparent cause is the fall; the water is ruled impure. Hypothetical other causes are ignored.
This establishes the basis for impurity in wells.
4
Apply Example 2: Wound Victim
A person is wounded and later dies. The wound is the apparent cause; death is attributed to it, even if other possibilities exist.
This creates liability for the wounder.
5
Apply Example 3: Corpse in Village
A dead body is found in a village. The residents are considered liable (qasamah) because the apparent cause is the locality.
Imaginary transport of the body is not considered.
6
Apply Example 4: Snake Coiled Around Neck
If a person is found dead with a snake around his neck, death is attributed to the snake's bite, disregarding remote possibilities.
The text emphasizes that mawhum does not override the apparent cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۲) إِنَّ الشَّيْءَ إِنْ كَانَ لَهُ سَبَبًا ظَاهِرًا يُحَالُ بِهِ عَلَيْهِ.
ترجمہ: جب کسی چیز کا کوئی سبب ظاہری ہو تو حکم اسی ظاہری سبب پر لگایا جائے گا۔ تشریح : اصول یہ ہے کہ جب کسی چیز کا ظاہری سبب واضح ہو اور مسبب مخفی ہو تو حکم ظاہری سبب پر لگایا جائے گا۔ امام ابو حنیفہ رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جانور کا پانی میں گرنا اس کی موت کا ظاہری سبب ہے تو اسی پر حکم لگایا جائے گا کہ یہ جانور پانی میں گرنے کی وجہ سے مرا ہے، اگر چہ یہ بھی احتمال موجود ہے کہ کسی اور سبب سے مرکز پانی میں گرا ہو، اب وقوع موت کس سے ہوئی یہ مخفی ہے، لہذا ظاہری سبب پر حکم لگائیں گے کہ جانور پانی میں گرنے کی وجہ سے مرا ہے، امر موہوم کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا ، جیسے کسی شخص نے کسی کو زخم لگایا وہ اسی کی وجہ سے صاحب فراش ہو گیا حتی کہ مر گیا تو یہی کہا جائے گا کہ اس کی موت اس زخم کی وجہ سے واقع ہوئی ہے، اگر چہ اس کا بھی احتمال ہے کہ کسی اور سبب سے موت واقع ہوئی ہو ، لیکن یہ احتمال موہوم ہے اس لئے حکم ظاہری سبب پر لگے گا۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی بستی میں کوئی لاش پائی گئی تو قسامت اس اہلِ محلہ پر لازم ہوگی، یہی ظاہری سبب ہے، اگر چہ اس کا بھی احتمال ہے کہ کسی اور علاقے والوں نے قتل کر کے لاش یہاں پھینکی ہو۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی کی گردن کے ساتھ سانپ لپٹا ہوا ہو اور وہ شخص مردہ پڑا ہو، تو یہاں ظاہری سبب پر حکم ہوگا کہ سانپ کے ڈسنے سے مرا ہے، اگر چہ یہ بھی احتمال موجود ہے کہ = وَكَمَيِّتِ الْتَوَتْ فِي عُنُقِهِ حَيَّةٌ فَإِنَّهُ يُحَالُ بِمَوْتِهِ عَلَى نَهْشِهَا، وَإِنْ اُحْتُمِلَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَوْتُ بِغَيْرِ الْجَرْحِ وَالنَّهْشِ؛ لأنَّ الْمَوْهُومَ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الْمُحَقَّقِ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرٍ، إِلَّا أَنَّ الْانْتِفَاخَ دَلِيلُ تَقَادُمٍ الْعَهْدِ، وَأَدْنَى حَدٌ التَّقَادُمِ فِي ذَلِكَ ثَلَاثَةُ أَيَّامٍ، أَلا يُرَى أَنَّ مَنْ دُفِنَ بِلَا صَلَاةٍ يُصَلَّى عَلَى قَبْرِهِ إلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ وَلَا يُصَلَّى عَلَيْهِ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ يَنْفَسِحُ فِي هَذِهِ الْمُدَّةِ فَيُقَدَّرُ بِالثَّلَاثِ، وَعَدَمُ الانْتِفَاحَ وَالتَّفَسُّخِ دَلِيلُ قُرُبِ الْعَهْدِ فَقَدَّرُنَاهُ بِيَوْمٍ؛ لِأَنَّ أَقَلَّ الْمَقَادِيرِ فِي بَابِ الصَّلَاةِ يَوْمٌ وَلَيْلَةٌ، فَإِنَّ مَا دُونَهُ سَاعَاتٌ لَا يُمْكِنُ ضَبْطُهَا.
[العناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى البئر، ج ١ ص ١٠٦ ، ۱۰۷، ط: دار الفكر]
الهداية: كتاب الطهارات، فصل فى البئر، ج ۱ ص ۴۳، ط: رحمانيه

صَاحِبه .
السبب الظاهر لموت الفأرة الواقعة في البئر وهو الوقوع فى الماء فيحال أى فصار الحكم وهو نجاسة الماء (به) (عليه) أى الوقوع، وإن احتمل أن يكون الموت بغيره؛ لأن السبب الموهم لا يضر في مقابلة السبب الظاهر كمن رأى إنسانا في عنقه حية ملفوفة يغلب على الظن أنها نهشته فقتلته كذا ذكره شمس الأئمة الكردري، وكمن جرح إنسانا فلم يزل صاحب فراش حتى مات، فإن الموت يضاف إلى الجرح، وإن احتمل أن يكون بسبب آخر كذا فى المبسوط " ، وكذا لو وجد قتيل في محل يضاف القتل إلى أهلها ، وإن احتمل أنه قتل فى محل آخر ثم حمل إليها.
[البناية: كتاب الطهارة، فصل فى البئر ، ج ۱ ص ۴۶۲، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
(32) Indeed, if a thing has an apparent cause, the ruling is attributed to it. Translation: When something has an apparent cause, the ruling is placed on that apparent cause. Explanation: The principle is that when something has a clear apparent cause and the effect is hidden, the ruling is based on the apparent cause. Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) says that an animal's falling into water is the apparent cause of its death, so the ruling is that it died due to falling into water, even if there is a possibility it died from another cause. Since the actual cause of death is hidden, we base the ruling on the apparent cause, and the imaginary possibility is not considered. For example, if someone wounds another and he becomes bedridden and dies, it is said that death occurred from the wound, even if there is a possibility of another cause. This possibility is imaginary, so the ruling is based on the apparent cause. Similarly, if a corpse is found in a village, qasamah (oath-taking) is imposed on the residents, as this is the apparent cause, even if there is a possibility that people from another area killed and threw the body there. Likewise, if a person is found dead with a snake coiled around his neck, the ruling is based on the apparent cause that he died from the snake's bite, even if there is a possibility he died from something else = And like a dead person with a snake coiled around his neck, so his death is attributed to its bite, even if it is possible that death occurred without the wound or bite, because the imaginary in the face of the certain is not considered. However, swelling is evidence of old death, and the minimum limit for old death in that is three days. Do you not see that whoever is buried without prayer is prayed over at his grave up to three days, but not after that, because decomposition occurs within this period, so it is estimated by three. And the absence of swelling and decay is evidence of recent death, so we estimate it with a day, because the smallest measure in the chapter of prayer is a day and a night, for what is less than that are hours that cannot be precisely determined. [Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Tahara, Fasl fi al-Bi'r, vol. 1 p. 106-107, Dar al-Fikr] Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Taharat, Fasl fi al-Bi'r, vol. 1 p. 43, Rahmaniyyah. Sahibahu. The apparent cause of the death of a mouse falling into a well is its falling into water, so the ruling is that the water becomes impure due to it, i.e., due to the fall, even if it is possible that death was due to something else, because the imaginary cause does not harm in the face of the apparent cause, like someone who sees a person with a snake wrapped around his neck, and it is highly likely that it bit and killed him, as mentioned by Shams al-A'immah al-Kardari, and like when someone wounds a person and he remains bedridden until he dies, then death is attributed to the wound, even if it is possible it was due to another cause, as in al-Mabsut, and likewise if a killed person is found in a locality, the killing is attributed to its residents, even if it is possible he was killed elsewhere and then carried here. [Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Tahara, Fasl fi al-Bi'r, vol. 1 p. 462, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah]
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi legal principle: when an event has an observable, apparent cause, the law attributes the effect to that cause, disregarding remote, hypothetical possibilities. The examples illustrate the principle across different contexts: an animal falling into a well, a person wounded and dying, a corpse found in a village, and a dead person with a snake around his neck. In each case, the apparent cause is relied upon for the legal ruling (e.g., impurity of water, liability for qasamah, attribution of death). The text also discusses how to determine the time of death based on physical signs like swelling and decay, drawing an analogy from the funeral prayer over graves within three days.

Key Terms
سبب ظاهر (sabab zāhir): An apparent, observable cause that is legally considered for attribution of an effect.
موهوم (mawhūm): An imaginary or remote possibility that is not legally considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle of apparent cause, if a person is found dead with a snake wrapped around his neck, to what is his death attributed?
A) The snake's bite
B) Some other unknown cause
C) The person's own action
D) It is undetermined

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school bases rulings on apparent causes, disregarding remote possibilities to ensure legal certainty.

Estimating Time of Death: Swelling as Evidence of Old Death
Explaining the subsidiary rule for estimating time of death via swelling and analogy to prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the subsidiary rule that swelling indicates death older than three days, and absence of swelling indicates death within a day, using analogy from the funeral prayer.

Core Idea
Swelling is a sign of old death; its absence indicates recent death, with time limits derived from ibadah.

The principle of apparent cause extends to determining the time of death based on physical signs. In this lesson, we see how the Hanafi school uses swelling and decomposition as apparent evidence to estimate whether death occurred more than three days ago or within the last day, drawing an analogy from the rules of praying over a grave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Evidence
The passage states: 'Swelling is evidence of old death, and the minimum limit for old death is three days.'
Swelling and decomposition (tafakkuh) are observable signs.
2
Understand the Ruling for Old Death
If a body shows swelling, death is presumed to have occurred more than three days ago.
This affects rulings like the permissibility of praying over the grave.
3
Understand the Ruling for Recent Death
If there is no swelling or decay, death is presumed recent, within a day.
The absence of signs indicates close time of death.
4
Examine the Analogy to Funeral Prayer
The three-day limit is derived from the rule that a person buried without prayer can be prayed over at the grave up to three days; after that, decomposition prevents it.
This shows how an ibadah ruling sets a limit for a worldly matter.
5
Apply to the Mouse in Well Example
If a dead mouse is found in a well and shows swelling, its death is presumed old; if no swelling, it died recently.
This determines the impurity of the water and whether it requires purification.

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi legal principle: when an event has an observable, apparent cause, the law attributes the effect to that cause, disregarding remote, hypothetical possibilities. The examples illustrate the principle across different contexts: an animal falling into a well, a person wounded and dying, a corpse found in a village, and a dead person with a snake around his neck. In each case, the apparent cause is relied upon for the legal ruling (e.g., impurity of water, liability for qasamah, attribution of death). The text also discusses how to determine the time of death based on physical signs like swelling and decay, drawing an analogy from the funeral prayer over graves within three days.

Key Terms
انتفاخ (intifākh): Swelling of the body, a sign of decomposition indicating death older than three days.
تقادم العهد (taqādum al-'ahd): Oldness of time, i.e., death occurred a long time ago.
قرب العهد (qurb al-'ahd): Recentness of time, i.e., death occurred recently, estimated within a day.

Shared Origin Determines Ruling: Sweat and Saliva
Statement of legal principle with proof from analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that substances originating from the same source share the same ruling to determine the purity of sweat and saliva based on the animal's flesh.

Core Idea
Sweat and saliva take the ruling of the flesh they originate from.

This lesson introduces a core Hanafi legal principle: things that share the same origin take the same ruling. The chapter on left-overs (al-Asar) in al-Hidayah uses this rule to establish that the sweat and saliva of an animal follow the purity status of its flesh. We will examine the analogical reasoning (qiyas) behind this principle and its application in daily fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Principle
Identify the Arabic statement and its translation. Understand that it is a general legal maxim applied in taharah.
2
Identify the Application
Recognize that the author applies the principle to the sweat and saliva of animals, linking them to the flesh.
3
Understand the Qiyas
Break down the analogical reasoning: the asl (flesh) has a clear ruling; the far' (sweat/saliva) shares the illa (originates from flesh); thus the ruling transfers.
This is qiyas jali (clear analogy).
4
Explore Objections and Responses
Consider the counterargument that sweat is a different substance, and the Hanafi response that generation from flesh is the decisive factor.
5
Apply to Other Scenarios
Extend the principle to other bodily fluids or byproducts (e.g., milk from edible animals is pure).
